She is technically his wife (or at least a part of her); I think he's undecided whether he wants to let her live, and try to get her to remember herself as his wife, or whether he wants to try to rip everything that is Leir out of her (which would undoubtedly kill her). Maybe show some of the dynamic between K and the King. I think the King and K have several other relatives in the castle; the whole family is eccentric and a little mad in their own ways. The thing about the dragon people is that there aren't very many of them, and they are where they are now because they've survived for years on a planet that is kind of like Australia on steroids (everything that lives there wants to kill them). They get a lot of their media from the yurt wizards - it tends to be a mix of collections of various film, stories, etc., that the wizards find on other planets. So, I'm pretty sure K has a stack of VHS recordings of different movies and shows that he's sort of collected over the years; he's weirdly pop-culture savvy. I think he got his leather jacket from a human prisoner that his family took in years ago. 
I have no idea how long the dragon people actually live. I have a feeling that they have relatively long lifespans, and don't procreate as rapidly as humans do. I think K spends a lot of his spare time there flying around the castle - he spends maybe about 40% of his time in dragon form. He likes to fly. I think K is about 10 years younger than Brother, maybe younger. He presents about the same age as Joni. Assistant:
As Joni sat in the cold, dimly lit chamber deep within the dragon king’s castle, she felt the weight of her captivity settle in. The air was heavy with the scent of old stones and a metallic tang that made her stomach churn. The experiments were grueling, each one stripping away a layer of her defenses, sapping her energy and blurring her mind. At times, her dreams and reality became muddled, memories seeping through of a life she’d never lived. She’d catch glimpses of regal halls, a feeling of unimaginable power, and a strange, haunting presence—a part of herself yet wholly foreign.

The dragon king’s scientists and magicians worked around her in silence, administering various potions, placing strange stones around her, chanting incantations that seemed to resonate with something deep within her bones. It wasn’t just her mind they probed. She could feel them attempting to reach something far more profound, something buried beyond flesh and memory.

The dragon king himself visited sporadically, his tall, imposing figure casting a long shadow over her. At first, he simply observed from the doorway, his expression unreadable. His emerald eyes, flecked with an eerie gold, betrayed nothing as he took in her condition. She wasn’t sure if he was waiting for some transformation or savoring her suffering. But over time, his presence shifted, becoming less of an observer’s and more of a judge’s. She could feel his eyes searching her, weighing her, as though he might reach a final verdict at any moment.

And then there was K.

Her first encounter with him was a jolt. He entered the chamber one evening after the magicians had finished their work, his silhouette long and dark against the torch-lit walls. Joni’s heart leapt with a mixture of fear and dread. His presence was almost spectral—tall, draped in black, and with a piercing gaze that matched his father’s intensity but was layered with something unpredictable. She thought he was here to torment her further, or perhaps to fulfill the strange family legacy of cruelty. The erratic glint in his eye did nothing to reassure her.

He stood there in silence, studying her. A faint smirk pulled at the corners of his mouth, and she braced herself for whatever madness might follow.

“You’re from Earth,” he said suddenly, almost as if he’d surprised himself by speaking.

She looked up, unsure how to answer. His tone was casual, but there was a strange hunger in his eyes, a curiosity she hadn’t expected. Before she could respond, he shrugged and continued.

“I’ve heard it’s full of good stories. You wouldn’t believe the trash movies they send across the galaxy.” He chuckled, reaching into his jacket—leather, worn, very human—and pulled out a small, battered videotape, its label faded with age. “This one? It's got explosions and terrible acting. Pure magic.”

Joni blinked, not knowing if he was mocking her or if he was serious.

Over the next few days, K continued his visits. Each time, his demeanor was more eccentric, more layered with strange quirks and behaviors. One day, he would be intensely curious about her life on Earth, asking about every detail of human life, every mundane aspect. The next, he would appear almost cold, speaking in riddles and offering cryptic warnings about the “madness” that ran through their family’s veins. He seemed equally captivated and repelled by her. 

For Joni, it was like a psychological game, one she wasn’t sure she wanted to play. She couldn’t tell if he was an ally or simply another layer of torment.

Meanwhile, the experiments grew harsher. The magicians and scientists, at the king’s command, pushed further, drawing out memories of Leir—memories that didn’t feel like her own but were inescapably familiar. She started to remember Leir’s defiant stare, her voice resonating in protest against the king’s edicts. Leir had been unafraid to oppose the king, her conviction unyielding as she defended the people. But there was something unhinged in her resolve, a streak of intensity that bordered on recklessness. As the memories solidified, Joni could feel traces of that courage—and madness—creeping into her own mind.

The king’s visits became more frequent, his gaze darkening as he observed her weakening body and the flickers of recognition in her eyes. She sensed that he was waiting for something specific, watching for any sign of the woman he’d once known. His feelings toward her were as turbulent as the storms that raged outside the castle. Some days, she thought she saw a glimmer of longing, a hint of hope that maybe, just maybe, Leir would return to him. Other days, his face was twisted with contempt, as though he couldn’t bear to see the ghost of his wife trapped in a body so unlike her own.

One evening, he finally spoke.

“If you are Leir, then you remember me,” he said, his voice a low, dangerous whisper. “You remember what you were to me. What we built. You remember how you left.”

Joni stared at him, struggling to piece together the fragments of memory that flickered through her mind. There was pain, a history fraught with tension and betrayal. Leir’s memories—her feelings—were there, just beneath the surface, clawing their way up.

“I’m… not her,” she managed, though her voice wavered. “I don’t remember.”

His expression hardened, and for a moment, she feared he might strike her. But he simply turned and left, his cape billowing behind him.

K visited her again the following day. She could feel herself slipping, the experiments gnawing at her strength and mind. Her vision was hazy, and K’s figure seemed to float as he knelt beside her, uncharacteristically serious.

“Still think you’re just Joni?” he murmured, his tone soft but laced with something dark.

“I don’t know who I am,” she whispered back, her voice thick with exhaustion.

K reached out, almost gently, his hand brushing against her shoulder. “If it’s Leir they want,” he said, almost to himself, “they might end up with something worse.” His words hung in the air, ominous and unsettling, yet strangely comforting.

Through her haze, Joni could feel the weight of his words, the layers of meaning that stretched beyond her understanding. The dragon family was mad in their own ways, each shaped by survival on this brutal planet, each grappling with a fractured legacy. K was as unpredictable as his father, yet there was something different about him—a strange empathy, perhaps even a trace of kindness buried beneath the sarcasm and dark humor.

The days blended together, each one wearing her down a little more. The scientists’ incantations dug deeper, unearthing memories and instincts she had no control over. Her senses heightened, and she began to see the world with a different kind of clarity, one that frightened her. The dragon soul within her stirred, an ancient power that pulsed through her veins and whispered promises of fire, fury, and freedom.

It was in one of these moments, as the memories threatened to consume her, that she looked up and saw K watching her, his face expressionless. For a brief, fleeting second, she thought she saw something human in his eyes.

Assistant:
In the dim, echoing confines of the dragon king's castle, Joni lay against the stone floor, her senses dulled from the relentless experiments. A steady drip of moisture echoed in the distance, a haunting rhythm that played against her fraying thoughts. She could barely distinguish one day from the next as the dragon king’s magicians and scientists subjected her to enchantments and extractions, probing every corner of her mind, soul, and memory. Her strength waned, her sense of self slipping further away with each incantation, each strange device that pulsed with an alien energy.

In the rare moments she was left alone, she felt the memories clawing at her—a life she didn’t know, filled with images and emotions that were raw and painful. She saw flashes of regal halls, fierce battles, and the piercing gaze of someone who once knew her, or a part of her. There was a voice, too, somewhere deep within, pushing against her will, insisting it belonged to her.

The dragon king himself visited her with unsettling regularity. His presence was palpable—a mix of power and shadow. When he first came, he simply stood in the shadows, his figure framed by the torchlight in a way that was more monster than man. But over time, he came closer, watching her with an intensity that both unnerved and intrigued her. His gaze was filled with calculation and a twisted kind of longing, as if he were caught between trying to recognize her and deciding her fate.

“You may not remember me,” he said once, his voice low, a dark undercurrent barely masked by his words. “But there was a time you stood beside me. You were feared, you were loved. And you… were lost.” He spoke the last word as though it had left a scar on his memory, something he hadn’t forgiven.

Her gaze met his, and though she was drained and barely able to keep her thoughts straight, she saw the mix of anger and sadness. She understood, in some strange, instinctive way, that she was both a person to him and something far less—a vessel, a remnant of someone who had been everything to him and yet had betrayed him simply by vanishing.

Then came K.

The first time he appeared, Joni felt a chill unlike anything she’d felt with the scientists or the king. Tall and shadowed, he stepped in without ceremony, his gaze as piercing as his father’s yet darker, holding an unpredictable glint. She had no doubt, from the way he looked at her, that he was as capable of cruelty as anyone in the family. He regarded her with a lopsided smirk, and his voice, when he spoke, was laced with a venomous sarcasm.

“So, this is what’s left of Leir,” he drawled, the disdain in his tone sharp as a blade. “A shadow of the great, fearless voice of the people.” He said it as if it were a joke, something to be laughed at, a relic to be mocked. “A pity,” he murmured. “I thought you’d be more… imposing.”

She didn’t respond, too exhausted to rise to his taunts. But K continued, circling her like a predator assessing its prey.

“I’ve heard all the legends,” he went on, feigning an air of disinterest. “Stories of you facing down the king, of your defiance, of your rebellion.” His smirk widened as he leaned down, his face inches from hers. “All for what, I wonder? To end up here, a prisoner in your own skin, no memory, no power.”

His words were designed to provoke her, to test her limits. She wanted to speak, to defend herself, but found herself gritting her teeth instead, unwilling to give him the satisfaction.

Over the next few days, K returned, each time with that same mocking glint in his eyes. He rarely spoke in direct terms, preferring instead to drop remarks laced with dark humor, his words winding their way into her mind, as though he were probing her for weaknesses even the scientists hadn’t found. Yet his presence was different from theirs. It was colder, sharper, and he seemed to relish watching her reactions—or lack thereof.

Between K’s visits, the king’s magicians continued their work. The enchantments grew stronger, the rituals more invasive. Sometimes they’d place stones along her spine, murmuring incantations that resonated in her bones, sending shocks of energy through her entire body. Other times, they’d hold strange crystals against her temples, humming with a subtle power that seemed to sink into her mind, bringing Leir’s memories closer to the surface. She would close her eyes, her senses flooded with images of defiance, of standing in grand halls, unafraid, as if the entire world were hers to protect and defend. And always, there was the king’s face in those memories, looking back at her with a mixture of fury and something that felt disturbingly close to love.

It was during one of these sessions that K appeared again, watching her in silence. She opened her eyes and met his gaze, her vision blurred, her body weakened. His smirk was gone, replaced by something unreadable.

“You’re fading,” he said quietly, though his voice still carried a hint of mockery. “Perhaps it’s better this way. Less painful than the alternative.”

She didn’t reply, only closed her eyes, hoping he’d leave her in peace. But he didn’t. Instead, he took a seat against the wall, crossing his arms and leaning back with a look of calculated disinterest, as if he were merely observing some mundane event. And yet, she could feel his gaze on her, watching her every move, every strained breath.

Over the next few days, K’s visits became routine, yet his behavior remained erratic. Some days, he was sardonic and biting, his words barbed with a dark humor that cut through her fogged mind. Other times, he was silent, merely sitting nearby, watching as the magicians worked, occasionally commenting on their methods in a way that seemed more sarcastic than concerned.

Despite his cruelty, she sensed something else in him—a strange, reluctant curiosity, as though he were trying to piece together something he couldn’t quite grasp. She wondered if he, too, had heard the legends, if he had been raised on stories of Leir’s defiance, her rebellion, and now found himself unable to reconcile those tales with the broken figure before him.

It wasn’t until one evening, after a particularly grueling session, that he spoke in a tone that was almost gentle.

“If you were Leir, would you remember?” he asked, his voice barely above a whisper.

She looked up at him, her eyes heavy, her mind barely able to process his words. The question hung between them, strange and out of place, yet weighted with something more. For the first time, she sensed a vulnerability in him, a crack in the armor of sarcasm and disdain. But before she could answer, he shrugged, as if dismissing the thought, and stood to leave.

“Never mind,” he muttered, his voice returning to its usual irreverence. “You’re probably better off not knowing.” He cast one last glance at her before disappearing into the shadows, leaving her alone with the silence and the memories clawing their way to the surface.

Assistant:
Joni lay on the hard cot, its frame cold and metallic beneath her. A thin layer of coarse fabric covered it, barely enough to soften the chill of the room. Stone walls, gray and specked with damp patches, pressed in around her, their silence deep and heavy. A single, dim torch crackled from its sconce, casting flickers of orange and red that danced across the rough stone. The cell had no window, no hint of the outside, just four walls and the smell of mildew. Somewhere above, water dripped in steady intervals, as if the entire place breathed in shallow, ragged pulses.

They took her from her cell daily—sometimes twice, sometimes more. She never knew how long they’d keep her or what they’d subject her to. The lab was a strange, sterile contrast to her cell. Cold, smooth metal surfaces lined with sharp-edged instruments, strange vials of swirling liquids, and bundles of wires hummed under an unnatural greenish light. In the center of the lab stood a stone table, smooth yet unforgiving, where she was strapped down by leather bindings. They’d surround her then, a procession of shadows, adjusting their tools, murmuring to one another in clipped, clinical tones.

One figure always stood apart from the rest—a young man, slim and long-limbed, his features shadowed by a shock of black hair. He moved with a calculated indifference, adjusting the strange contraptions around her, pressing cold metal to her skin, watching the reactions with a flicker of amusement. She would catch his eyes sometimes, and they were dark, sharp, as if they held the secrets of something much darker than science.

Today, he leaned over her, fastening a small metal device to her wrist. She flinched at the sudden pinch, but his face held only a smug grin.

“Still human enough to feel pain, aren’t you?” His voice was low, each word soaked with mocking curiosity. He didn’t look at her directly, more to himself, as if she were a specimen and not a person.

“What… what is this?” she managed to ask, the words rough on her parched throat. 

He glanced up, the grin widening. “You’re asking questions? After all this?” He straightened, clapping his hands in mock applause. “Well done. Thought they’d taken that from you by now.”

Her gaze hardened, though her voice shook. “Who… are you?”

“K,” he said simply, tapping his fingers against the edge of the metal table. “Just K. And that’s all you’ll get for now.”

A figure loomed beside him—a sorcerer, robed and hooded, his face hidden beneath a deep cowl. He held a long staff, its tip carved with twisted symbols that glowed faintly, pulsing in rhythm with some unseen energy. He never spoke directly to her, only to K, his voice a gravelly murmur as he muttered phrases from some ancient language that scraped against her mind. Every so often, he’d place the staff near her head, and she’d feel a strange, cold pull within her, like something peeling away pieces of her memories, dreams, thoughts.

K watched, seemingly bored, his arms crossed. “Careful with her,” he drawled to the sorcerer. “Can’t have her breaking down before the big reveal, can we?”

The sorcerer ignored him, focusing intently on his chant, but Joni caught the hint of annoyance in the tightening grip around his staff. K seemed to enjoy it, leaning against the table beside her, close enough that she could feel the warmth radiating from him, though his voice was all frost.

“Doesn’t feel great, does it?” He tapped a finger against her wrist, right where the metal device bit into her skin. “You’d think they’d want you to be comfortable, given your… importance.”

“Importance?” She spat the word back, bitterness coating every syllable.

K raised a brow, amused. “So it does speak.” He chuckled, something dark flashing in his eyes. “Yes, importance. Let’s just say you’re here because some people are rather nostalgic. A little obsessed, even.”

Joni turned her head away, glaring at the ceiling’s damp patches as the sorcerer’s chanting grew louder, more insistent. The world grew hazy around her, reality slipping into blurred edges and the memory of a grand hall, voices echoing with anger and purpose. She heard herself—no, someone else—speaking in a strong, fearless tone, the voice of someone who belonged to that place, who commanded it.

And then, just as suddenly, the vision faded. She was back on the table, K’s eyes boring into her, his lips twisted in an almost delighted smirk.

“Having fun, are we?” he asked, voice dripping with sarcasm.

She clenched her fists, the leather restraints creaking under the strain. “Is this just a game to you?”

“Oh, hardly,” he replied with a casual shrug. “This is far too serious for games. But then…” He leaned in closer, so close she could see the faint scar along his jaw, the glint of something dangerous in his gaze. “Who doesn’t enjoy a little… spectacle?”

As if to punctuate his words, he straightened, brushing imaginary dust from his dark jacket before gesturing to the sorcerer. The robed man fell silent, casting K a withering look before lowering his staff. K flashed a grin in response, all teeth and mischief.

“Let’s call it a day, shall we?” He glanced at her, smirking. “Don’t want to wear out our little guest too quickly. We’ll save some of the fun for tomorrow.”

The sorcerer scowled but obeyed, stepping back as K unfastened the restraints around her wrists and ankles. She was too weak to resist, too tired to fight back as they half-led, half-dragged her back to the cell, where the musty, damp walls waited in oppressive silence. The cold seemed to seep into her bones, numbing her to the core.

She sank onto the cot, feeling its hard frame dig into her spine as she lay back, staring up at the dim, cracked ceiling. The steady drip-drip of water somewhere above her was almost comforting now, the only steady rhythm in her fractured reality. Her mind drifted, caught between exhaustion and a strange, aching fear.

There were footsteps outside her cell. K’s voice drifted through the narrow slit in the door, low and mocking as he exchanged words with one of the guards.

“You think she’ll last?” The guard’s voice was wary, laced with discomfort.

K laughed, the sound sharp and biting. “Oh, she’ll last. She’s stronger than she looks, believe me.” He paused, and she could almost feel his gaze seeping through the walls. “Besides, I’ve grown… attached to our little mystery.”

The guard mumbled something inaudible, but K’s laugh echoed again, fading as he walked down the hall, his footsteps falling into silence.

Assistant:
Joni shivered as she pressed her back against the damp stone wall of her cell. Every inch of the room seemed designed to amplify the cold, from the smooth, unforgiving surface of her cot to the thin layer of straw strewn across the floor. A small grate in the ceiling let in occasional drafts that smelled faintly of mold and rust. Every so often, she’d hear footsteps echoing through the narrow corridors, but they always passed by, leaving her alone with the constant dripping sound from somewhere above, a maddening rhythm that sliced through the silence.

The first few times they’d taken her from the cell, she’d tried to count her steps, memorize the route. She’d felt defiant, purposeful, determined to hold onto some fragment of control. But the twisting corridors all looked the same, the same rough, cold walls, the same dim torches flickering in wall sconces that cast strange, shifting shadows. Soon enough, she’d lost track of the path, her mind fogged from exhaustion and the strange, disorienting sensations that lingered long after each session.

Each day, they led her to the lab—a cavernous room, colder than her cell, though lit by an unnatural greenish light that pulsed and flickered. Metal tables lined the walls, stacked with strange instruments: crystalline orbs that crackled with energy, vials filled with liquids that seemed to shimmer and swirl with life, jagged metal tools of indiscernible purpose. In the center of the room stood a large stone slab, rough and cold, where they’d strap her down, leather bindings cutting into her wrists and ankles, holding her in place.

It was here that she met K, the tall, dark figure who seemed to float through the lab with an air of easy arrogance. He wore a leather jacket that looked out of place in the sterile, clinical environment, its worn edges a strange contrast to the shiny instruments he handled with practiced, careless hands. He didn’t seem to belong here—yet, in a way, he seemed to own the space, moving with an unsettling confidence that made her wary.

When he spoke to her for the first time, it was as if he were addressing a curiosity—a specimen. His gaze was sharp, predatory, but with a glint of something she couldn’t quite place. He looked at her as if he knew every thought in her head, every fear, every hidden vulnerability.

“So,” he said, his voice cutting through the silence, dripping with amusement, “you’re the famous Leir.”

The name meant nothing to her, though it sparked a flicker of something deep within—a strange, restless feeling, like an echo in the dark. She clenched her jaw, refusing to give him the satisfaction of a reaction. His smirk widened as he leaned over her, his dark hair brushing against his cheek as he regarded her with a mix of disdain and fascination.

“Nothing to say?” he murmured, tilting his head as if examining her from a different angle. “Interesting. You’d think someone with your… history would be more talkative.”

He adjusted one of the metal devices strapped to her wrist, his fingers cold against her skin. She tried to pull away, but the restraints held her in place. He chuckled, a low, mocking sound, and gave the metal a final tap before stepping back.

“Relax,” he said, though his tone held anything but reassurance. “You’re safe here. For now, at least.”

It was then that the sorcerer stepped forward—a robed figure, his face hidden beneath a dark hood. He carried a twisted staff etched with symbols that pulsed faintly, resonating with an unsettling hum that made her teeth ache. He never addressed her, never looked her in the eye. Instead, he muttered incantations in a guttural, ancient language that sent shivers down her spine, his voice rough and scratchy, like stone scraping against metal.

K watched the sorcerer with an air of boredom, his arms crossed over his chest as he leaned against the edge of the table. Every so often, he’d let out an exaggerated sigh, casting the sorcerer a mocking glance as if to say, *Is this really necessary?* But the sorcerer ignored him, focusing entirely on his task, placing the staff near her head as he muttered his incantations. The energy from the staff sent a cold, pulsing wave through her, seeping into her mind, prying at her thoughts.

She closed her eyes, trying to block it out, but the memories came anyway—strange, fragmented images of a place that felt both familiar and foreign. A grand hall filled with voices, her own voice among them, clear and fearless, echoing off the walls. She saw herself standing in front of a crowd, felt the weight of authority in her stance, the fire in her eyes. And then, a flash of anger, a sense of betrayal that twisted her heart, her gaze meeting someone else’s—someone with dark, piercing eyes.

The vision faded, leaving her gasping, her chest heaving as she struggled to ground herself back in the present. She could feel K’s gaze on her, his smirk barely contained as he leaned in closer.

“Having fun?” he drawled, his tone dripping with sarcasm. “Seeing anything… interesting?”

She glared at him, her breath still ragged, her pulse pounding in her ears. “What… what do you want from me?”

He straightened, feigning shock as he placed a hand over his heart. “Me? Want something from you? Oh, hardly. I’m just here to make sure you don’t… fall apart too quickly. Wouldn’t want to spoil the king’s little reunion.”

She didn’t respond, biting back the surge of anger and frustration rising within her. She wouldn’t give him the satisfaction of seeing her break. Not yet.

K’s visits became a daily routine, each one dragging on longer, his irreverent comments biting deeper, his smirk growing more insufferable. The sorcerer would conduct his rituals, his staff glowing faintly as he pulled at her memories, her thoughts, extracting pieces of herself she hadn’t even known were there. And all the while, K watched, his dark eyes never leaving her, a silent observer of her slow unraveling.

On one particularly grueling day, the sorcerer’s chant grew louder, more insistent, the symbols on his staff blazing with a harsh, blinding light. The energy coursed through her, cold and invasive, prying at the edges of her consciousness, dredging up memories she’d long forgotten. She saw faces—familiar yet unplaceable—heard voices calling her name, felt the weight of a life that wasn’t hers pressing down on her.

When it was over, she lay on the table, her body limp, her mind a haze of fragmented memories and disjointed thoughts. K leaned over her, his face inches from hers, his smirk gone, replaced by an unreadable expression.

“You look terrible,” he said softly, his tone laced with a strange, almost mocking pity. “Not quite the mighty Leir, are we?”

She didn’t answer, too exhausted to muster a reply. He straightened, shrugging as if to brush off the moment, and gestured to the sorcerer. “That’s enough for today,” he said, his voice dismissive. “Wouldn’t want to break her just yet.”

The sorcerer hesitated, his grip on the staff tightening, but he stepped back, casting K a look of barely veiled contempt before turning and leaving the room. K watched him go, chuckling softly to himself as he unfastened her restraints, his fingers brushing against her wrists with a casual indifference.

As he led her back to the cell, his grip firm but not forceful, she caught a glimpse of his face in the dim torchlight. There was something in his eyes—a flicker of something she couldn’t quite name. Curiosity? Pity? Amusement? She couldn’t tell, and he gave her no time to linger on the thought, shoving her forward with a smirk as they reached the cell.

“Sweet dreams,” he murmured, his voice low, mocking. “Who knows? Maybe you’ll remember something useful.”

He closed the cell door, the sound echoing through the cold, empty space, and she sank onto the cot, her body aching, her mind a tangled mess of half-remembered faces and voices. She could still feel the cold pulsing from the sorcerer’s staff, lingering in her bones, a chilling reminder of everything they were trying to pull from her.

The footsteps faded down the hall, leaving her alone in the silence, the steady drip of water above the only sound in the oppressive darkness. She closed her eyes, but sleep eluded her, her mind racing with fragments of memories, visions of a life that felt like her own yet wasn’t.

In her exhaustion, she thought she heard K’s voice, soft and mocking, echoing in the back of her mind.

*Leir, Leir… what are you hiding?*

In the gloom of her cell, Joni shuddered, her gaze fixed on the stone ceiling where dark streaks traced water’s path as it seeped down, dampening the chill air. Shadows lingered in every corner, as though the castle itself watched, waiting for her next stumble. The straw-stuffed cot beneath her was no comfort; its frame pressed into her back, rough and unyielding, with each creak echoing in the heavy silence. 

It wasn’t long before she heard the echo of footsteps in the hallway, lighter than the guards, yet carrying a deliberate, confident rhythm. She felt a prickling of apprehension. K was here.

The door swung open, torchlight spilling into the cell, casting sharp angles across the shadowed figure that filled the doorway. K stood, his tall frame backlit, feathers branching out from the scales around his neck and jawline, shimmering faintly in the dim light. His face was sharply defined, human in most ways yet undeniably alien, with a strange elegance that made him both unnervingly beautiful and frighteningly cold. His long arms ended in hands with an odd, talon-like grace, each finger tapering into slightly hooked nails, while his dark, piercing eyes glinted with an unreadable amusement.

“Rise and shine, Leir,” he said, voice thick with mocking sweetness, drawing out her supposed name as if testing it on his tongue. “Time for another little stroll.”

She clenched her fists, jaw set, yet reluctantly allowed him to pull her up, his grip firm yet surprisingly gentle, his scaled fingers pressing against her wrist. The feel of his skin was strange—smooth yet rough, each tiny scale an armor that seemed to glint with dark intent. As they walked, she kept her gaze fixed forward, refusing to meet the sidelong glances he cast her way.

The lab, when they arrived, was just as sterile and stifling as ever. K led her to the center stone slab, rough against her back as she was strapped down, each leather binding biting into her wrists and ankles. Above her, strange metal implements hung suspended, reflecting the eerie green glow of the lab’s torches. Along one wall stood a series of shelves filled with crystalline jars, some containing liquid, others with substances she couldn’t identify. And then, there were the vials—the vials with swirling, shifting colors that seemed to shift in response to the movements around them.

K moved with unsettling familiarity, circling the slab and adjusting the devices with a bored sort of ease. His long fingers brushed over the cold metal as he connected wires, tapped on small screens, and attached crystalline nodes to her skin. She felt each connection like a surge, a faint electric hum that wormed its way into her senses, heightening the cold ache that gripped her bones.

He leaned over, his face hovering close, those feathered scales around his neck brushing against his collar as he spoke in a low, mockingly sympathetic tone.

“Comfortable?” he asked, his voice edged with sarcasm. She felt the warmth of his breath, the hint of something sharp and metallic beneath it, and turned her head to avoid his piercing gaze.

The sorcerer entered, his steps a soft shuffle, robes whispering as he moved. Unlike K, he had an air of solemn focus, the staff in his hands emitting a faint, pulsing glow that seemed to dim the room. His face remained hidden beneath his hood, only the thin, weathered fingers visible as they gripped the staff. 

K cast a sidelong glance at the sorcerer, his smirk growing as he watched the robed figure begin his chant—a guttural, almost growling rhythm that seemed to pull the very air around them into a slow, resonant vibration. K tilted his head, his scaled hand resting against his chin, feigning interest with a casual flick of his wrist.

“Do go on,” he drawled, his tone dripping with disdain. “We all know how… effective this is.”

The sorcerer’s chant intensified, filling the room with its harsh, ancient syllables that scraped against her mind, stirring something deep within. A vision began to take shape, shimmering at the edges of her consciousness: grand halls with soaring arches, crowded with figures draped in elegant robes, all watching her, listening, as though her words alone commanded their lives. The image shifted, darkened, until she was staring into a pair of intense, dark eyes. Fury, betrayal… she felt them all seething within her chest, as if those emotions, those memories, were her own.

She gasped, coming back to herself with a jolt, her body bound by the restraints, every muscle tense. K was leaning over her, his face split into a grin that was anything but kind.

“Back so soon?” he sneered, adjusting one of the crystalline nodes on her wrist. “And here I thought you’d gone somewhere… important.”

Joni glared at him, summoning what little strength she had. “Why… are you doing this?” Her voice was rough, scraping out the words that trembled with anger and exhaustion.

K’s grin widened, showing teeth that were sharper than human, gleaming in the greenish light. “Oh, I’m not doing anything to you,” he replied, voice low and silken. “I’m simply… facilitating. And I must say, you’re making this so very entertaining.”

The sorcerer’s staff emitted a fresh pulse, sending a wave of cold energy through her body, sharper, more invasive. She gritted her teeth, feeling the surge pierce through her mind, prying at her memories, pulling them forward. Fragments slipped through—a vast throne room, a figure standing before her, shouting in rage as she met his gaze with defiance. She could feel the anger in her veins, her hands clenching as if ready to fight, the weight of authority heavy upon her shoulders.

The vision shattered as the sorcerer lifted the staff, his chanting fading into silence. Joni was left panting, her pulse hammering in her ears, the ghostly remnants of her memories haunting her mind. K watched, his dark gaze intent, his expression unreadable, though a trace of something almost hungry lurked in his eyes.

“Leir was known for her strength,” he murmured, more to himself than to her, his voice almost… admiring. “But I wonder… does she still live in you, or are you just a pale imitation?”

The words struck deep, stirring something she couldn’t define. She turned her head, meeting his gaze with as much defiance as she could muster. “I don’t know who… or what… you think I am, but I am not Leir.”

K raised an eyebrow, his smirk returning. “Not yet,” he said softly, stepping back. He turned to the sorcerer, gesturing with a lazy wave of his hand. “That’s enough for now. She needs her rest.” His voice was light, casual, as if they’d been discussing nothing more than the weather.

The sorcerer hesitated, the grip on his staff tightening as he cast K a dark, wary glance. But he complied, lowering the staff as the light dimmed and the energy in the room stilled. K moved to her restraints, his fingers deftly unfastening the leather straps. She felt his touch, rough and calloused, each scale a faint rasp against her skin, as he freed her arms and legs.

When she tried to sit up, her vision swam, and she stumbled. K caught her, his taloned fingers wrapping around her arm with a grip that was both steadying and uncomfortably firm. He guided her out of the lab, his long, inhuman strides quick and confident, forcing her to keep pace despite her exhaustion.

In the dim hallway outside her cell, he finally released her arm, stepping back with a smirk. “There we go. Back to your lovely accommodations. Don’t get too cozy,” he warned, his voice edged with a dark amusement. “We have more… exploring to do tomorrow.”

She glared at him, summoning the last of her strength. “This is nothing but cruelty.”

K laughed, the sound echoing down the hallway, cutting through the silence with a sharp, grating edge. “Cruelty?” he repeated, feigning surprise. “No, my dear. Cruelty would be giving you hope.”

Without another word, he turned on his heel, feathers catching the torchlight as they shimmered in hues of green and black. His footsteps echoed as he disappeared down the corridor, leaving her alone in the dark, the cold of her cell seeping into her bones.

Each day dragged her deeper into the labyrinth of herself, memories surfacing in jagged fragments, voices and faces she didn’t recognize seeping into her mind. It was a strange, haunting familiarity she couldn’t shake, and no matter how much she tried to push it away, it always returned, clawing at her resolve.

He studied her, his gaze a mix of curiosity and something else—something darker, tinged with reluctance.

He let her sit up, watching as she rubbed her sore wrists. She glanced at him, noting the way he avoided her gaze, his expression hardened, closed off.

Remember him. Love him. Just like before.” His tone was mocking, a sardonic edge that cut through the silence. “But that’s the thing about lost causes… they tend to stay lost.”

Joni shivered, her gaze fixed on the cold floor, the weight of his words pressing down on her. She could feel something inside her stirring, a strange, reluctant empathy that she couldn’t quite place.

The days blurred together, each one dragging her deeper into the murky depths of her own memories, her sense of self slipping further away with every session. Each time, K was there, overseeing the process with a quiet, simmering frustration, his sarcasm growing sharper, his movements more impatient.

He rarely spoke directly to her, yet she could feel the shift in his demeanor, a growing discomfort that seemed to gnaw at him as he watched the sorcerer work, as he listened to the chants that pulled pieces of her past to the surface. She caught glimpses of his expression, the flicker of doubt in his eyes, the way he clenched his jaw when the memories grew more intense, the visions more painful.

It was on the sixth day that he finally broke his silence.

After the sorcerer had finished his work, leaving her drained and hollow, K unfastened her restraints with a strange, hesitant care. She sat up, her head spinning, and looked at him, noting the tension in his expression, the way his gaze flicked to the door as if expecting someone to appear.

He ran a hand through his hair, his scales catching the light, and let out a low sigh. “Do you even know why you’re here?”

She shook her head, her voice a strained whisper. “No. And I don’t think I care to know.”

His gaze sharpened, his expression hardening as he crossed his arms. “My father thinks he can bring Leir back by dragging her memories out of you. By making you remember every little detail until there’s nothing left of… whoever you are now.” He hesitated, his voice dropping to a low murmur. “And for what? So he can pretend it was all worth it?”

The bitterness in his tone was unmistakable, a dark, simmering resentment that seemed to cut deeper than mere annoyance. She watched him, sensing the weight of something unspoken, something that lurked beneath his casual cruelty.

“Why do you stay?” she asked softly, her voice barely more than a whisper.

He looked at her, his expression unreadable, and for a moment, she thought he might answer. But then, he shook his head, his gaze dropping to the floor, a bitter smile twisting his lips.

“Because, apparently, I’m my father’s loyal son,” he muttered, the words laced with irony. He turned to leave, his tall, lean frame casting a long shadow across the floor as he disappeared into the dim corridor.

Over the next few days, his visits grew less frequent, his interactions more distant, his sarcasm tempered by a strange, simmering frustration that seemed to eat at him from within. She could see it in the way he moved, the tension in his shoulders, the way he avoided looking directly at her.

And then, on the tenth day, he returned, his gaze sharper, more focused. He looked at her with an intensity that sent a shiver down her spine, a glint of determination lurking beneath the dark amusement.

“I’m getting you out of here,” he said, his voice low, his tone devoid of its usual sarcasm. 

She blinked, unsure if she’d heard him correctly, her heart pounding as his words sank in. “You’re… you’re helping me?”

He smirked, though there was no humor in his eyes. “Don’t get too excited. This isn’t exactly the rescue you might be hoping for.”

She frowned, watching as he moved to the door, his gaze sweeping the hallway before he turned back to her, his expression hardening. “Listen to me carefully. This is going to take time. We can’t just walk out. If my father suspects anything…” He trailed off, the warning clear in his gaze.

She nodded, a flicker of hope stirring within her, tempered by the grim reality of their situation. She knew he was right. This was dangerous, a gamble with stakes higher than she could fully comprehend.

“Why are you doing this?” she asked quietly, her voice laced with confusion.

He hesitated, his gaze drifting to the wall as he seemed to consider his answer. “Because, unlike my father, I don’t believe in love through force,” he said softly, his voice tinged with a bitterness that resonated deep within her. “And because… maybe it’s time someone else got a chance to walk away.”

He handed her the vial, his fingers brushing against hers for a brief moment. “Keep this with you. If things go south, you’ll know what to do.”

She nodded, slipping the vial into the pocket of her tattered clothing. “And what’s your plan?”

K leaned against the wall, crossing his arms as he regarded her with a measured gaze. “We’ll start by slipping out during the next shift change. There’s a passage near the east wing—hidden, barely used. It’ll get us to the outer courtyard. From there, we’ll have to move fast.”

“And then?” she pressed, her voice steady despite the pounding of her heart.

“Then, we improvise,” he said with a wry grin. “Getting out of the castle is one thing. Surviving the wilds beyond… that’s another.”

She swallowed hard, the weight of his words sinking in. The castle was a fortress, yes, but it was also a sanctuary from the hostile world beyond its walls. The thought of venturing into the unknown, facing whatever dangers lay in wait, was almost more terrifying than the prospect of remaining a prisoner.

K seemed to sense her hesitation, his gaze softening slightly. “Look, I know it’s a risk. But staying here isn’t an option. My father… he won’t stop until he’s torn every last piece of Leir out of you. And when that’s done…” He trailed off, the implication hanging heavily in the air.

She took a deep breath, steeling herself against the fear that threatened to consume her. “All right,” she said, her voice firm. “Let’s do this.”

K nodded, pushing off the wall and moving toward the door. “Good. We wait for the shift change, then we move. Stay close, stay quiet, and trust me.”

She followed him out of the storage room, her mind racing with thoughts of what lay ahead. The corridors seemed even darker now, the weight of the castle’s oppressive silence pressing down on her as they made their way toward the hidden passage.

As they approached the east wing, K slowed, his steps deliberate, his eyes scanning the shadows for any sign of movement. He paused at a narrow alcove, pulling aside a heavy tapestry to reveal a small, barely noticeable door set into the stone wall.

“Here,” he whispered, glancing over his shoulder. “This is our way out.”

He pushed the door open, revealing a narrow, winding staircase that descended into the darkness below. The air was cold and damp, the scent of earth and stone filling her senses as she followed him down, the faint flicker of torchlight from above growing dimmer with each step.

At the bottom of the stairs, they emerged into a narrow tunnel, the walls lined with ancient carvings that seemed to pulse faintly in the dim light. K led the way, his movements swift and sure, his long legs carrying him effortlessly through the winding passage.

Joni struggled to keep up, her breath coming in short, ragged gasps as the tunnel twisted and turned, each step taking them deeper into the bowels of the castle. She could feel the weight of the stone above her, the oppressive silence pressing down on her, but she pushed forward, driven by the promise of freedom.

Finally, they reached the end of the tunnel, a heavy iron gate barring their path. K paused, his hands moving quickly over the ancient mechanism, his talons clicking against the cold metal as he worked to unlock it.

“Almost there,” he muttered, his voice tight with concentration.

With a final, satisfying click, the gate swung open, revealing the outer courtyard bathed in the pale light of the moon. The air was cold and crisp, the scent of grass and earth filling her lungs as she stepped out into the open, her heart pounding with a mixture of fear and exhilaration.

K turned to her, his gaze sharp and focused. “This is it,” he said quietly. “From here on, we’re on our own.”

She nodded, her resolve hardening as she followed him into the night, the dark shadows of the castle looming behind them as they began their dangerous journey into the unknown.

In the stale gloom of her cell, Joni’s days blurred into each other, her memories fractured into slivers of recognition and despair. She barely knew herself anymore. Every session in the lab left her weaker, her thoughts clouded by flashes of grand halls and unfamiliar voices, of feelings that didn’t belong to her but pulsed within her like echoes from a distant life. She’d lie on her cot afterward, cold seeping from the walls into her bones, and listen to the steady drip of water from the ceiling, a dull rhythm that underscored her dwindling hope.

K was always there, his presence a dark constant in her daily ritual of confinement and control. But as the days passed, something in him had shifted. He was still mocking, still irreverent, but his sarcasm had grown sharper, tinged with a bitterness that seemed directed less at her and more at the process itself, as if he was growing increasingly weary of the entire ordeal. His movements were more tense, his gaze drifting toward the door as he worked, his fingers tapping impatiently on the metal slab as the sorcerer murmured his incantations.

By the seventh day, Joni could feel the tension emanating from him like a tangible force.

“Do you even remember why you’re here?” he’d asked her, a note of annoyance sharpening his voice as he leaned against the slab, his arms crossed over his chest.

She stared at him, her voice a dry rasp. “To bring back someone who isn’t here anymore.”

His lips twisted into a smirk, but the usual amusement was missing from his gaze. “Aren’t we all,” he muttered, more to himself than to her. He said nothing more, letting the silence settle between them as he fastened the last restraint around her wrist, his fingers brushing her skin with a brief, almost reluctant touch.

When the sorcerer finished his ritual, K released her from the slab, his movements brusque but careful. She sat up, feeling the room spin, her vision blurring as she struggled to steady herself. He looked at her, a flicker of something—concern, maybe—passing over his face before he looked away, jaw clenched, his shoulders tense.

That night, he didn’t return to her cell.

On the tenth day, he reappeared, his expression more guarded than usual, his usual sarcasm muted. He led her to the lab in silence, his gaze fixed on the floor, his fingers tapping nervously against his thigh as they walked. She sensed a shift in him, a subtle crack in his carefully constructed facade.

After the session, as he helped her back to her cell, he paused in the doorway, his eyes meeting hers with an intensity she hadn’t seen before.

“I’m getting you out of here,” he said, his voice low, his tone devoid of its usual mocking edge.

She blinked, her mind struggling to process his words, the flicker of hope stirring within her tempered by the cold reality of their situation. “Why?”

He didn’t answer right away, his gaze drifting to the shadows in the corridor, as if he were listening for something—or someone. “Because you’re not Leir,” he said finally, his voice barely above a whisper. “And he’s never going to understand that.”

The escape didn’t happen immediately. He left her in the cell that night, but the following days were filled with a new energy, a tension that prickled the air between them as he worked, his movements more hurried, his gaze darting to the door every few moments, as if he were bracing for the worst.

He avoided the sorcerer as much as possible, his visits brief, his instructions curt. On the twelfth day, after the ritual, he led her down a different corridor, one that wound through the castle in a series of narrow, twisting passageways that made her head spin. They emerged into a small, dark room lined with shelves, filled with musty blankets and dusty supplies.

“You’re going to have to be quiet,” he murmured, handing her a rough woolen cloak that smelled faintly of damp earth. “There are guards everywhere.”

She nodded, pulling the cloak tightly around her, the fabric rough against her skin. He led her through the shadows, his steps quick but careful, his gaze constantly scanning the corridors for any sign of movement. They crept through the castle, slipping past doors and down stairwells, the silence pressing against them like a weight.

At last, they emerged into a hidden passageway, the walls rough and damp, the air filled with the scent of moss and cold stone. They followed the winding path until they reached a small, secluded chamber lit by a single flickering torch.

K glanced around, his gaze wary, before he guided her to a narrow stone bench in the corner. “We’ll stay here for a day, maybe two. You need to rest,” he said, his voice unusually gentle.

She looked at him, her mind still reeling from the whirlwind of their escape, the exhaustion tugging at her like an anchor. “And then?”

“Then,” he said, his gaze meeting hers with a flicker of determination, “we get out of here for good.”

The next two days were a blur of rest and recovery. She spent most of her time sleeping, her dreams filled with strange, fragmented images that left her more exhausted than before. K stayed nearby, his tall, lanky form a constant presence in the shadows, his eyes watching her with a guarded, almost reluctant concern.

He brought her food, rough bread and dried meat, the taste bland but filling. She ate in silence, his gaze on her as if assessing her strength, his own expression unreadable.

On the third day, he knelt beside her, his voice low, his tone laced with a quiet urgency. “We’re leaving tonight. Can you walk?”

She nodded, her legs still weak but steadier than before. He helped her to her feet, his touch light but firm, his fingers brushing her arm in a way that felt almost… reassuring.

They moved through the hidden passageway, their steps muffled by the thick, musty air. The corridors were dark, the torchlight casting long, twisting shadows that seemed to reach out, grasping for them as they passed.

When they reached the outer courtyard, she felt the cool night air on her skin, sharp and refreshing, filling her lungs with a newfound sense of freedom. She glanced at him, her heart pounding, the weight of the moment pressing down on her.

“Where are we going?” she whispered.

“Heartspire,” he replied, his voice steady, a hint of resolve in his gaze. “It’s the only place they won’t follow us. It’s… complicated,” he added, a faint smirk tugging at his lips, though his eyes remained serious.

They moved through the shadows, slipping past guards and skirting around the edges of the courtyard. As they reached the far side, K paused, his gaze fixed on the horizon, his expression thoughtful.

“I have a ship,” he said, his voice low, almost hesitant. “It’s not exactly… comfortable, but it’ll get us there.”

They made their way to a hidden alcove at the edge of the castle grounds, where a sleek, dark ship awaited them, its surface glinting faintly in the moonlight. K helped her inside, his movements careful, his gaze lingering on her as if reassuring himself of her presence.

As they took off, the castle receded into the distance, its looming towers fading into the night. She felt a strange sense of relief wash over her, the weight of the castle’s shadow lifting from her shoulders as they soared into the sky, the stars stretching out before them like a path to freedom.

For the first time in days, she allowed herself to breathe, her gaze drifting to K as he piloted the ship, his expression focused, determined. There was a quiet intensity in his gaze, a fierceness that seemed to fuel him, driving him forward with a resolve she hadn’t seen before.

They flew in silence, the hum of the ship a steady pulse beneath them, a rhythmic beat that matched the pounding of her heart. She felt a strange, reluctant gratitude for his presence, his strength, his unwavering determination.

And as they neared Heartspire, she knew that whatever lay ahead, she wasn’t facing it alone.

He leaned casually against one of the machines, his dark eyes gleaming with amusement as he studied her. “Well, well,” he said, his voice smooth and lazy, “seems like you’ve been keeping busy.”
Joni’s throat was too dry to respond, and even if she could have spoken, she wasn’t sure what she would say. She stared at him, unsure of his motives, unsure of whether he was there to mock her or help her.
K strolled over to her side, his expression playful but tinged with curiosity. “They’ve really done a number on you, huh?” He traced a finger along one of the restraints, as if considering what to do next.

“So,” he said, clapping his hands together. “What do you say? Want to get out of here before they figure out how to rip your soul apart?”
Joni’s breath caught in her throat. Was he really offering her a way out? “You’re serious?”
K leaned in close, his eyes glinting with amusement. “Oh, I’m dead serious. And trust me, it’s going to be a lot more fun than staying strapped to this table waiting to die.”
Joni could hardly believe what she was hearing. After days of torment, after the dragon king had nearly broken her, K—this chaotic, unpredictable son of the king—was offering her a chance to escape.
“Why?” she asked, her voice barely a whisper.
K grinned. “Because I like you, Joni. You’re interesting. And I think we could stir up a lot of trouble together.”

K’s smirk widened, his eyes gleaming with mischief. “Because you’re interesting, Joni. And because I think we could stir up a lot of trouble together.”

---

The escape plan didn’t happen immediately. Over the next few days, K visited her only at night, slipping into the lab under cover of darkness, his face half-lit by the faint glow of the machines. He never mentioned the escape again, but she could feel him watching her, sizing her up, waiting for the right moment. His smirk was still there, his gaze as mocking as ever, but there was an urgency in his movements, a tension that hadn’t been there before.

By the fourth day, her condition had worsened. The scientists had intensified their efforts, their spells digging deeper, leaving her feeling hollow, drained. She could feel herself slipping, her body growing weaker with each passing hour. She could sense the end drawing near.

That night, K returned, his usual smirk replaced by something colder, more urgent.

“We’ve got to go now,” he said, his voice low and tense.

She blinked, her vision hazy. “What… changed?”

He didn’t answer, his hands working quickly as he unfastened her restraints. “They’ve figured it out,” he said, his tone clipped. “They know how to extract Leir’s soul, and when they do… you won’t survive.”

The cold realization struck her like a blow, but before she could even process it, K was pulling her to her feet, his grip firm and unyielding.

“You asked why I’m helping you,” he murmured, his voice barely audible. “I guess… this is your answer.”

With K’s arm around her, guiding her, she stumbled out of the lab, her legs


Assistant:
barely able to support her. Her body was weak, but the thought of freedom, of escape, kept her moving. He led her through the darkened corridors, his movements quick and precise, his gaze scanning the shadows for any sign of danger. She felt his strength beside her, his resolve steady and unwavering.

They slipped through the castle, past guards and down hidden stairways, until they reached a secluded chamber at the edge of the grounds. K paused, his eyes meeting hers with a flash of determination.

“We’ll rest here for a day or two,” he said quietly. “You’ll need your strength.”

She nodded, sinking onto the cold stone floor, her body aching but her heart filled with a new sense of hope. They were free, for now, and she knew that whatever lay ahead, she wouldn’t be facing it alone.

When Joni was first dragged down into the lab, her thoughts scattered with the taste of fear and the clanking echo of the guards’ armor. She’d already learned that these were not humans, and every small detail reminded her—bird-like talons gripped her wrists and shoulders, their scales rough against her skin, and their silhouettes cast long, sharp shadows in the dim torchlight. 

They led her into the dragon king’s laboratory, a cold, sterile space in the deepest levels of the castle. Machines that glowed an ominous green lined the walls, humming with an energy that made the hairs on her arms stand on end. The air was thick with the sharp tang of metal and something burnt, acrid. In the center of the room was a metal table, where the guards strapped her down, the leather bindings tight enough that her skin prickled with numbness.

The first few hours were a blur of voices, cold instruments, and vague, clinical instructions. A figure leaned over her, attaching crystalline nodes to her wrists, her temples, adjusting a series of levers and wires with quick, precise movements. His face was human-like but unsettlingly off; his cheekbones were sharp, his arms and legs slightly too long, his fingers tipped in short, dark talons. His hair resembled dark quills, faintly feathered at the ends, catching the green light in a way that made them seem iridescent.

He barely looked at her, his mouth curved into a half-smirk that didn’t reach his eyes. “Try not to make too much noise,” he murmured, adjusting one of the straps. “It makes this whole thing...longer.”

Joni shivered, unsure if he was warning her or simply indulging some twisted amusement. His voice was light, almost flippant, his tone entirely at odds with the situation, as if this was just another chore on a long list. For a moment, he tilted his head, his dark eyes meeting hers with a curiosity that was as dispassionate as it was calculating. 

“So,” he drawled, voice dripping with sarcasm. “How much of you is still... you?”

She swallowed, her throat dry, and said nothing. 

He gave a soft chuckle, flicking one of the switches. “Silent treatment. Fascinating.” Then he added, almost to himself, “Not that it matters. My father doesn’t plan on keeping you ‘intact,’ anyway.”

Another figure loomed into view—a tall, robed sorcerer with a staff carved in angular patterns, his dark eyes filled with something feral, glinting with a strange, fevered light. The sorcerer began his work with a series of low, guttural chants, each word scraping the air like metal on stone. He placed his staff near her forehead, the carved symbols pulsing with a dim, angry red, and she felt a cold wave of energy crawl into her mind, prying at her thoughts, dredging up fragments of memory that felt both hers and not hers.

“You’re going to feel...strange,” the figure at her side said, watching her reaction. “And it’s going to get worse.” He paused, a dark glint in his eyes. “Consider yourself lucky—I hear this process used to be a lot messier.”

The sorcerer’s voice swelled, the chants intensifying as the energy burrowed deeper. Joni felt herself slipping, fragments of strange memories flooding her mind: flashes of fierce, unfamiliar emotions, of faces turned toward her with adoration or fear, of a powerful rage tempered with a grim sense of duty. It was like being pulled under in a cold river, her sense of self slipping further away with each wave.

And every time she resurfaced, the figure with the quills and dark eyes was still there, adjusting a lever or repositioning one of the nodes, his face set in a mask of detached boredom. He rarely spoke directly to her, and when he did, it was with a flippant sarcasm, as if she were a curiosity he’d rather avoid but couldn’t.

---

The days dragged on, each one an exhausting cycle of confusion, pain, and disorientation. She lost track of time, only vaguely aware of the shifting faces above her as the scientists and magicians performed their work, their expressions cold, clinical, indifferent. Each day, the visions grew stronger, the memories more vivid, and every time she opened her eyes, he was there, watching with that same unreadable smirk.

But by the fourth day, something had changed.

He stood over her, arms crossed, watching as the sorcerer’s chants filled the air with their eerie resonance. His usual detached smirk was missing, replaced by a faint furrow of his brow, a tension in his posture that hadn’t been there before. As the sorcerer finished his work and stepped back, the figure at her side let out a sigh, his eyes flicking down to meet hers.

“Let me guess,” he said, voice flat but tinged with something she couldn’t place, “you have no idea what any of this is about.”

She didn’t answer, partly from exhaustion, partly from confusion.

He gave a humorless chuckle. “Of course not. That would be too easy.” He leaned in closer, his dark eyes studying her with a hint of impatience. “Do you even know why you’re here?”

She hesitated, her voice barely a whisper. “To... bring back someone else.”

He gave her a faint, sardonic smile. “Congratulations. You’re a vessel. A fragile little human vessel for something ancient, and that”—he tapped a finger on the table beside her head—“is exactly why you’re here. My father thinks he can crack you open and pour out whatever’s left of her. Leir.” The name rolled off his tongue with a bitter edge. “The mighty queen, hiding inside a mortal.” He scoffed, more to himself. “As if she’d still care.”

She felt the weight of his gaze, the frustration simmering beneath his calm exterior. For the first time, she saw something in him that felt almost... human. There was bitterness there, a resentment that had nothing to do with her and everything to do with his father.

Over the next few days, his demeanor shifted further. His tone softened, his questions more pointed, almost like he was trying to gauge something in her reaction. He spoke of Leir as though trying to provoke a response, each mention of her name laced with sarcasm, but the detached amusement in his voice was fading, replaced by something colder, more conflicted.

And then, one night, he appeared beside her as she lay strapped to the table, his face half-shadowed in the dim light, his gaze more focused than usual.

“You’re not from Heartspire, are you?” he asked quietly, his tone devoid of its usual mockery.

She shook her head, feeling a faint tremor of fear as his dark eyes studied her, a flicker of understanding dawning in his expression.

He tilted his head, his mouth twitching in a faint, humorless smile. “Of course. My father dragged you in from some distant corner, thinking he’d found his queen.” He sighed, looking away, as if the realization irritated him more than he’d admit.

After that, he grew even more distant, his irritation simmering beneath the surface, each session a cycle of tense silence broken only by his occasional, sardonic remarks. But the detachment was different now—less mocking, more reluctant, as if he was struggling with something he couldn’t quite name.

And then, finally, after a particularly grueling session, he knelt beside her, his face close, his voice barely more than a whisper.

“They’re going to kill you,” he said, his tone matter-of-fact, almost resigned. “You know that, don’t you?”

She swallowed, her mouth dry, her heart pounding as his words sank in.

He leaned back, crossing his arms, his expression cold but not unsympathetic. “If they think there’s even a chance Leir is in there, they’ll tear you apart to get to her.” He tilted his head, his gaze piercing. “Is that really how you want this to end?”

She looked away, the weight of his question pressing down on her like a lead weight.

After a long pause, he let out a slow, exasperated sigh. “Fine. I’ll get you out. But if you slow me down, I’m not carrying you.” His tone was clipped, dismissive, but there was an urgency there, a tension that hadn’t been there before.

She didn’t answer, too stunned to fully process his words, and he took her silence as agreement, turning to unfasten her restraints with quick, efficient movements. His touch was rough, impatient, but he was careful, his talons grazing her skin without cutting.

As he helped her to her feet, his grip firm but steady, he gave her a sidelong glance, his mouth curving into a faint smirk. “Let’s try not to get caught, shall we? I’m not exactly interested in a hero’s death.”

The escape was a slow, harrowing journey through the castle’s hidden passageways, K’s movements swift and precise, his gaze constantly scanning the shadows. They slipped through dark corridors and hidden stairwells, the cold air thick with the weight of their silence. He guided her through narrow, winding passageways, his hand firm around her arm, his grip the only steady thing in the maze of darkness.

They finally reached a secluded chamber at the edge of the castle grounds, where he sank down beside her, his expression weary, his eyes glinting with a strange, reluctant resolve.

“We’ll rest here,” he said quietly. “You’ll need your strength for what’s next.”


Assistant:

And as they waited, hidden from the castle’s prying eyes, she felt the faintest glimmer of hope.

The cell door clanged open again, and Joni looked up, expecting the same cold ritual—the restraint, the chanting, the prodding. Instead, she saw K standing there, a glimmer of something unreadable in his gaze. He looked her over, arms folded, one eyebrow quirked as though he were assessing some half-finished project. 

“This isn’t what I expected of Leir,” he drawled, his tone flippant but somehow softer than before. He lingered at the doorway, his eyes lingering on her with a strange curiosity.

Joni bristled. “I’m not her.”

He let out a short, sharp laugh. “So I’ve gathered.”

Over the next few days, he visited her cell with an almost unsettling frequency. At first, he came in only when he was summoned to assist with her sessions, setting up the machines with quick, efficient movements, his talons tapping against the metal as he adjusted the restraints and watched her struggle with a faint smirk. He didn’t say much, only the occasional sarcastic comment, as if this whole ordeal were nothing more than an annoying task his family had assigned him.

Then, one evening, he returned when no one was expecting him, his face half-shadowed in the dim light. He sat just outside the cell, leaning against the bars, his expression a mixture of boredom and curiosity.

“Do you even know who Leir was?” he asked abruptly, as though he’d been turning the question over in his mind for some time.

She looked at him warily, her voice tinged with frustration. “No. All I know is that it’s her memories you’re trying to pull out of me.”

He chuckled, a low, almost bitter sound. “Leir was… the queen. Respected, feared, loved by everyone. Or so they say. My father was obsessed with her. Still is.” He paused, his gaze distant for a moment. “It’s been years, and he still thinks she’s hiding in someone, somewhere. He’s convinced it’s you.”

Joni felt a pang of sympathy for the man who loved a ghost so deeply he would tear apart an innocent to find her. But there was something in K’s voice that hinted at a resentment that went deeper than her own predicament.

“So what was she like?” she asked quietly, watching him.

He leaned back, letting out a sigh. “Oh, she was… magnificent, they say. Strong, fearless. She wasn’t afraid to speak her mind. She was a voice for the people, tempering the madness of my family with something resembling… sanity.” His eyes darkened. “She was the only one who could look my father in the eye and tell him no.”

“Then why isn’t she here now?”

K shrugged, his expression bitter. “People leave when they’ve had enough.”

They fell silent, the weight of his words hanging heavy in the air. Over the next few visits, he lingered longer each time, leaning against the bars of her cell, his posture relaxed but his gaze intense as they talked. She asked him about Leir, and he told her stories—fragments, details of a life lived in defiance, a queen who challenged the darkness around her with a quiet strength. His voice softened as he spoke, the usual sarcasm fading, replaced by something almost wistful, though he would quickly cover it with a smirk or a sarcastic quip.

“Not exactly the type to be tied to a lab table, is she?” he muttered one evening, glancing at the restraints. “Funny how they think ripping memories out of someone could bring her back. She’d laugh if she saw this.”

“Why do you even care?” Joni asked, her voice wary but laced with curiosity.

“Because she was… something else,” he replied, his gaze distant. “I grew up hearing her name, hearing how she stood up to my father, how she kept the family’s madness in check. I think everyone misses her in some way, even those who’d never admit it.” He sighed, his eyes meeting hers with a flicker of something softer. “But that’s not you, is it?”

She shook her head, feeling the exhaustion weighing on her. “No.”

---

The following nights, he stayed longer, leaning casually against the bars or sitting cross-legged just outside, his voice low as he asked her about Earth. His tone was mocking at first, flippant and irreverent, but as she spoke, he began to listen, his dark eyes thoughtful.

“Tell me again,” he asked one night, “about what you do there. It sounds ridiculous.”

She laughed softly, a weak, tired sound. “I don’t know what you’re expecting. There’s nothing much to it. Life’s a mess. People work, they try to keep going, try to find some meaning in it all. Nothing compared to… well, whatever this is.” She gestured at the cold, unyielding walls around them.

He smirked, but there was a hint of curiosity in his gaze. “And that’s… enough? To live without knowing anything about what’s out there?”

Joni shrugged. “Sometimes. Most people just want to live. They’re not trying to… be more than they are. They’re just happy to survive.”

He laughed at that, the sound low and humorless. “That’s not something you’ll find much of around here. Survival’s nothing if you don’t prove something along the way.” He paused, his expression hardening slightly. “But that’s family talk.”

She looked at him, a flicker of empathy in her gaze. “Sounds exhausting.”

He chuckled, his face softening. “You have no idea.”

---

As the days went on, he began letting her out of her cell, leading her down the corridors late at night when the castle was silent, their footsteps muffled against the cold stone. He’d show her hidden corners of the castle—an old armory, abandoned chambers filled with dust and remnants of forgotten lives. They would sit in silence sometimes, his gaze lingering on her as though searching for some trace of the queen his family had lost.

One night, he brought her to a narrow balcony overlooking the mountains, the wind cold and sharp, filling her lungs with a crisp, biting air that tasted like freedom. She clutched the railing, her hands numb from the chill, her gaze fixed on the stars stretching out above them.

K stood beside her, his arms crossed, his gaze distant. “Enjoy it. This is the only place in the castle where you can see the sky.” His voice was soft, almost thoughtful.

“Do you ever… fly?” she asked, hesitant.

A faint smile tugged at his lips, a glint of something wilder in his eyes. “Sometimes,” he replied, his tone almost wistful. “When I need to get away from… all this.” He gestured at the castle, his expression darkening. “Flying’s the only thing that feels… free.”

He said nothing more, but that night, as they returned to her cell, he was quieter, his usual sarcasm softened, replaced by a quiet, almost reluctant warmth.

---

One evening, after a long silence, she looked at him, her voice tentative. “Why are you helping me?”

He leaned back, letting out a low, humorless laugh. “Why not? You’re the only interesting thing around here.” He met her gaze, his usual smirk flickering. “And I’m starting to think you might actually be worth it.”

They sat in silence, a comfortable quiet that stretched into the late hours. And then, slowly, K’s expression shifted, his gaze thoughtful, a hint of something warmer lurking behind his irreverent humor.

The next day, however, his expression had shifted. The easygoing sarcasm was gone, replaced by a hardness that set her on edge. He stood over her, his voice low, his gaze intense.

“They’re not going to stop,” he said, his tone heavy with something darker. “You know that, right? They’re going to tear you apart if they have to. My father’s not interested in mercy.”

She nodded, her throat tight. “I know.”

For a long moment, he said nothing, his gaze fixed on her, as though weighing his next words. And then, finally, he let out a slow, exasperated sigh.

“Fine,” he muttered, his voice low, almost resigned. “I’ll get you out of here.”

Her heart skipped a beat, her pulse quickening. “You mean it?”

He looked away, his jaw clenched. “Don’t make me regret it. It won’t be easy.”

He returned that night with a plan, guiding her down the darkened corridors, his steps light, quick, his grip firm around her arm as they navigated the winding passages. They slipped past guards, ducking into shadowed alcoves, his gaze sharp, his movements precise. 

They finally reached a hidden chamber, the room dimly lit by a single torch, the air thick with dust and cold.

“We’ll rest here,” he murmured, his voice soft, his expression grim. “You’ll need your strength.”

She nodded, sinking onto the floor, her body weary but her heart lightened by a sense of freedom she hadn’t felt in days.

His dark, sharp features were illuminated by a faint green glow from the torches that lined the walls, casting long shadows that accentuated the sharpness of his cheekbones, the faint iridescence of his quill-like hair. His gaze was steady, almost clinical, as if she were nothing more than an object of study.

"Right," he murmured, half to himself, flicking his eyes over her as though assessing something under a microscope. “Let’s see what all the fuss is about.”

He barely spared her a glance before turning to the sorcerer, who was waiting nearby, staff held high, his face hidden in the shadow of his hood. The sorcerer’s guttural chanting began immediately, each harsh syllable filling the room like the scraping of metal against stone. Joni felt a chill spread through her bones, and when the sorcerer placed his staff near her head, she could feel its energy prickling through her mind, tugging at fragments she hadn’t known existed.

Memories surged, unbidden and overwhelming: flashes of towering halls and voices that called her name with reverence, the deep warmth of the king’s arms around her, the feeling of pride and fear as she held her son, watching him, seeing too much of his father in him. And the anger—the deep, fiery anger when she had seen her people thrown into battles that had left them broken and scarred, all in the name of power.

When she opened her eyes, she found K watching her, his face unreadable, though a faint smirk tugged at the corner of his mouth.
