Assistant:
The days in the dragon king’s fortress stretched on like an endless dream—sharp and vivid yet tinged with a nightmarish unreality. Every morning, Joni was dragged from her cell, down a twisting maze of corridors, and into the cold, sterile laboratory where the sorcerer and his assistants awaited. The sorcerer’s spells were relentless, invasive things that coiled into her mind and dredged up memories she couldn’t place. The life of someone else began to seep through her consciousness—flashes of grandeur, fierce loyalty, and love blended with bitterness. 

Each time she closed her eyes, she saw fragments of Leir’s life, woven through with emotions too intense, too unfamiliar to be hers. Memories of holding court, of fierce arguments, of love that was both consuming and painful. It was like being haunted by a ghost that was trying to claim her, and the more she tried to push it away, the stronger it seemed to grow.

Then came the physical changes. At first, they were so slight she thought she was imagining them—a slight shimmer along her arms, a faint texture under her skin. But as the spells grew more powerful, the changes became undeniable. Silvery scales appeared along her forearms and shoulders, smooth but unyielding, catching the light with a faint, almost ethereal glow. Her eyes felt different, sharper somehow, and sometimes, when she looked into the small mirror in her cell, she thought she saw something almost otherworldly staring back.

Every night, she returned to her cell, exhausted and disoriented, her mind struggling to hold on to her own identity. She tried to ignore the changes, to brush them off, but each time she looked at her hands, each time she felt the smooth, hard scales under her fingertips, the reality of her situation sank deeper.

---

On the third night, a figure appeared in the shadowy hallway outside her cell. She had grown used to the sounds of the guards’ heavy boots, the sorcerer’s clipped footsteps, but this visitor moved differently—quiet, purposeful, with a faint air of impatience. When he finally stepped into the faint light, she saw a face that was sharper, younger, yet somehow no less unsettling than the king’s.

K, the dragon king’s son, stood before her, his eyes dark and calculating, his mouth curled into a smirk. He watched her for a long moment, his gaze trailing over her like she was something fascinating and grotesque in equal measure.

“You look… well,” he said, his tone laced with sarcasm. He leaned against the bars, tilting his head to study her more closely. “Or at least… well enough.”

Joni sat up, clutching her arms, her fingers tracing the scales that had begun to line her skin. She could feel his eyes on them, could almost sense his curiosity, sharp and almost predatory.

“What do you want?” she asked, her voice tired, wary.

K shrugged, a faint glimmer of amusement in his eyes. “I was curious. My father has been waiting for a… result, but it seems you’re taking your time.”

“Your father wants something that isn’t there,” she replied, struggling to keep her voice steady. “He wants Leir, but I’m not her.”

K’s smirk widened, his gaze never leaving her. “Are you sure? Because from where I’m standing, you’re looking more and more like her every day.”

Joni glanced down at her arms, at the silvery patches that covered her skin, a shiver running through her. She felt caught between two worlds, her own self slipping further away with each passing day, replaced by someone else—someone powerful, ancient, and deeply wounded.

“It’s… not me,” she murmured, almost to herself.

K’s expression softened for a moment, something almost like sympathy flickering in his eyes. But he covered it quickly, leaning back with an exaggerated sigh. “Maybe. Or maybe you just don’t want to accept it.”

She glared at him, her anger flaring despite her exhaustion. “Why should I have to accept any of this? I never asked for it.”

K’s smirk faded, and he looked away, his gaze turning distant, thoughtful. “No,” he said softly, almost to himself. “I suppose you didn’t.”

For a moment, they were silent, an unspoken understanding settling between them. She could feel the weight of his gaze, the strange mixture of curiosity and something else—something more complicated.

---

After that night, K began visiting her more frequently, sometimes slipping into her cell in the dead of night, other times lingering just outside, his presence a strange comfort amid the endless monotony of her captivity. Their conversations started out tense, guarded, but over time, they grew more relaxed, more familiar. 

He would sit outside her cell, cross-legged and casual, his wings folded against his back, watching her with an almost lazy curiosity as they talked. He seemed to relish the conversations, his usual sarcasm giving way to genuine interest, though he would never admit it.

One evening, as they sat in the dim light of the cell, he looked at her with a faint smirk, his gaze flickering over the scales on her arms. “So, what’s it like?” he asked, his tone half-teasing, half-genuine. “Being… not quite yourself?”

Joni looked down at her hands, at the strange shimmer of the scales, and felt a familiar ache in her chest, a mixture of frustration and sadness. “It’s… hard,” she admitted quietly. “I feel like I’m losing myself, like… like I’m becoming someone else.”

K tilted his head, his expression thoughtful, almost sympathetic. “Maybe you’re just… finding another part of yourself,” he murmured. “A part you didn’t know was there.”

She shook her head, her fingers tracing the scales with a mixture of fear and fascination. “I don’t want this. I don’t want to become someone else.”

K watched her for a moment, his gaze softening, and for the first time, she thought she saw a flicker of something vulnerable in his eyes. “You know,” he said quietly, “dragons don’t fight who they are. They… embrace it. Whatever it is.”

She looked up, meeting his gaze, a mixture of curiosity and apprehension swirling in her chest. “Do you… spend a lot of time as a dragon?”

He shrugged, a faint smile tugging at his lips. “About half the time. There’s… a freedom in it. The world feels different when you’re flying, the air sharper, the sky… bigger. It’s hard to explain.”

Joni listened, entranced, her mind drifting to the memories that haunted her—the sensation of wings, of the wind rushing over her scales, of the world stretching out below her like an endless tapestry. She couldn’t tell if the memories were hers or Leir’s, but they filled her with a strange longing, a desire she couldn’t quite name.

“Do you think… I could feel that one day?” she asked, her voice barely more than a whisper.

K’s smile softened, his gaze gentle, almost kind. “Maybe,” he murmured. “If you let yourself.”

They sat in silence, the weight of his words settling over her, and for the first time, she felt a glimmer of hope, a sense that maybe, just maybe, she could find a way to reconcile the two parts of herself without losing who she was.

---

One night, as they talked, she asked him about Leir, her voice hesitant, almost afraid. “Who… who was she? Really?”

K’s expression turned distant, a flicker of sadness passing over his face. “Leir was… someone who believed in things. She didn’t care for power, not like my father. She wanted… something else. Something bigger.” He paused, his gaze flickering to her, a faint smile tugging at his lips. “She loved him once, you know. But he… he didn’t see her. Not really. He saw something he could own.”

Joni felt a pang of sorrow, a hollow ache that echoed through her own memories, and for the first time, she understood the depth of the dragon king’s obsession, the twisted love that had driven him to this madness.

“Do you think… he’ll ever understand?” she asked softly.

K looked away, his jaw tightening. “No. He’s… he’s lost himself in this. He’s too far gone.”

They sat in silence, the weight of his words settling over them, and for the first time, Joni felt a strange kinship with him, a shared understanding that went beyond words.

---

In the days that followed, K’s visits grew more frequent, his presence a constant, reassuring warmth in the cold, indifferent walls of her cell. They talked for hours, their voices soft, their laughter echoing in the darkness, a fragile thread of connection that bound them together.

But every time he left, a new worry gnawed at him. His father’s plans were progressing, the spells growing stronger, more invasive, and he knew, with a sickening certainty, that it was only a matter of time before the king found a way to extract Leir’s essence completely.

One night, he slipped into her cell, his usual smirk replaced with a look of grim determination. He sat beside her, his gaze steady, his voice quiet.

“They’re getting close,” he murmured, his voice barely audible. “If… if they find a way to bring her back, you… you won’t survive.”

Joni looked at him, her heart pounding, a mixture of fear and hope swirling in her chest. “Then… what are you going to do?”

He hesitated, his gaze flickering with doubt, and for the first


Assistant:
time, she saw the weight of his loyalty, the struggle between his family and the strange, fragile connection he had found with her. He looked away, his jaw clenched, a bitter smile tugging at his lips.

“I… don’t know,” he admitted, his voice barely more than a whisper. “This… this isn’t easy for me. If I help you, it would mean betraying everything I’ve ever known.”

Joni reached out, her hand resting on his, a gesture of understanding, of solidarity. “I don’t want to ask you to betray your family. But… I don’t want to die either.”

They sat in silence, their hands entwined, the weight of his decision pressing down on them both, and for the first time, he felt the true depth of his choice, the lines he would have to cross to save her.

---

For several nights, K wrestled with his decision, the weight of it gnawing at him, tearing him apart. He visited her cell every evening, their conversations growing more strained, more fragile, as though both of them knew that their time was running out.

Then, one night, he entered her cell, his face pale, his expression resolute.

“I’ve made my decision,” he murmured, his voice steady, his gaze fierce. “We’ll leave. But we’ll have to be careful. If they find out… neither of us will survive.”

Joni looked at him, her heart pounding, a surge of gratitude and relief flooding her chest. “Thank you,” she whispered, her voice filled with emotion.

K managed a faint smile, a flicker of warmth in his eyes. “Don’t thank me yet. This… this isn’t going to be easy.”

Over the next few days, they made their plans, their conversations quiet and tense, every step laced with the knowledge that one wrong move could spell their end. They mapped out the castle’s hidden passages, rehearsed their route, every detail etched into their minds.

And when the night finally came, when they slipped through the darkened corridors, their footsteps soft and measured, Joni felt a strange sense of peace, a quiet assurance that, no matter what happened, she wasn’t alone.

As they stepped out into the cool night air, leaving the fortress behind, she looked up at him, a question in her gaze, but he only smiled, a small, genuine smile that held a glimmer of something she hadn’t seen before—something close to hope.

Assistant:
Joni’s world had become an endless haze of pain and exhaustion. Each day, each session in the lab, drained her further, pulling her deeper into a fog where her own identity slipped in and out of reach. She no longer knew what was real and what was a remnant of Leir’s memories—those vivid, intense flashes of another life that gripped her mind in moments of weakness.

She remembered standing in a throne room, the air heavy with the weight of responsibility, of loyalty and betrayal. She felt the ache of love turned sour, of promises broken and power warped. Sometimes, as the spells wound around her, she saw herself—or rather, saw *Leir*—arguing with the dragon king, her voice rising in frustration, her words cutting and filled with bitterness.

But the visions were disorienting, bleeding into her own memories, clouding her mind until she couldn’t tell if she was Joni or Leir. She was adrift, caught between two lives, two selves, neither of which felt fully her own.

The transformation continued, unrelenting. The scales spread across her skin, shimmering patches on her arms, shoulders, and neck, hard and unyielding. Her vision sharpened, her senses seemed heightened, and her own reflection became foreign, alien. She hardly recognized herself, her human self slipping away, piece by piece.

By the time K finally made his decision, Joni was barely able to stand. She lay in her cell, her breaths shallow, her body heavy, almost numb. When she closed her eyes, she could still feel the faint prickle of magic, the relentless pressure of the sorcerer’s spells, tearing at her mind, trying to reach the last, hidden pieces of Leir.

She didn’t know how much longer she could hold on. Her spirit felt frayed, worn thin by the constant assault, and a part of her feared that if the experiments continued much longer, there would be nothing left of her to save.

---

K slipped into her cell one final time, his face tense, his eyes sharper than she’d ever seen them. He moved to her side, crouching down, his gaze searching her face, his hand hovering just above her shoulder as though afraid to touch her.

“Joni,” he murmured, his voice uncharacteristically gentle, almost hesitant. “Can you hear me?”

She blinked, her vision blurred, her mind struggling to pull itself back from the fog. “K…?” Her voice was weak, barely more than a whisper.

He let out a slow, measured breath, a flicker of relief crossing his face. “Good. You’re still here.”

She managed a faint, weary smile. “Not… sure how much longer.”

A shadow passed over his expression, his jaw tightening. “You’ll make it,” he said, his voice laced with determination. “I’m getting you out of here. Tonight.”

She wanted to feel relief, hope, something, but her body was too worn, her mind too frayed. She simply nodded, letting him help her to her feet, his arm steadying her as she leaned heavily against him.

The walk through the castle was a blur of shadows and whispered steps. She was only vaguely aware of the corridors, the low hum of magic that pulsed faintly through the walls, the distant sound of footsteps as they navigated through the hidden passageways. K moved with a practiced precision, his movements quick and purposeful, his arm tight around her shoulders, guiding her through the maze-like corridors with a confidence that reassured her, even in her dazed state.

Finally, they reached a small, secluded doorway, half-hidden by ivy and shadow, leading out into the open air. The night was cool, the breeze gentle against her face, and for a moment, the simple sensation of fresh air felt almost overwhelming. She breathed it in, the coolness sharp in her lungs, grounding her, reminding her of the world outside the castle walls.

K led her a short distance from the fortress, guiding her down a winding path that led into a small, hidden grove surrounded by trees. It was quiet here, the air still, a stark contrast to the cold, sterile atmosphere of the lab, and as they settled down in the grass, she felt a faint sense of peace, fragile but real.

K eased her down onto the ground, his hand lingering on her shoulder as he looked her over, his eyes filled with a mixture of concern and frustration.

“You look… worse than I thought,” he said, his tone a mix of sarcasm and genuine worry.

She managed a faint chuckle, the sound barely audible. “Thanks. You’re too kind.”

He smirked, though there was no humor in his eyes. “I aim to please.” He hesitated, his gaze flickering over her face, the faint shimmer of scales on her skin. “How are you… feeling?”

She closed her eyes, letting out a slow, shaky breath. “Like I’m barely here. Like… like I’m losing myself.”

He nodded, his expression grim. “I know. I could see it happening.” His voice softened, a hint of guilt threading through his words. “I should have done this sooner.”

She looked at him, her gaze steady despite her exhaustion. “You… had to make a choice. I understand.”

For a moment, they sat in silence, the weight of everything unsaid hanging between them. She could feel his hand resting on her shoulder, steady and warm, a strange comfort in the midst of her fading strength.

“Joni,” he murmured, his voice low, almost hesitant. “Do you… do you even know who you are anymore?”

She closed her eyes, her thoughts a tangled mess of memories, emotions, fragments of a life that wasn’t hers. “I… I don’t know,” she admitted softly. “I feel like… like there’s nothing left of me. Just… pieces of her.”

He let out a slow breath, his hand moving to gently grasp her arm, his thumb brushing over the scales with a strange tenderness. “She was strong. Fierce. But she… she wasn’t you.” His gaze softened, his voice barely more than a whisper. “You’re someone else. Someone… important. And I don’t want to lose that.”

She looked up at him, her heart pounding, a flicker of something warm and real stirring in her chest. “K…”

He shook his head, a faint, almost rueful smile tugging at his lips. “Don’t get sentimental on me. You’ll make me regret this.”

She laughed, the sound weak but genuine, and for a moment, the tension between them eased, replaced by a quiet, shared understanding.

---

Over the next two days, K stayed with her in the grove, bringing her water and food, watching over her as she rested. He spent most of the time in his dragon form, the quiet majesty of his scales glinting in the moonlight, his wings folded elegantly against his back as he kept watch over the area.

She found herself watching him, fascinated by the way he moved, the way his eyes gleamed in the darkness, the way his scales seemed to shift and shimmer with each movement. He was beautiful, in a strange, otherworldly way, and she felt a flicker of awe as she observed him, a curiosity she hadn’t felt in days.

One evening, as they sat together under the stars, she looked at him, her gaze thoughtful. “What’s it like?” she asked, her voice soft, almost reverent. “Being… like that?”

He tilted his head, his eyes glinting with faint amusement. “Being a dragon?” He shrugged, his wings shifting slightly. “It’s… freeing. Powerful. There’s a sense of… belonging. Like I’m part of something bigger, something ancient.”

She nodded, her hand unconsciously tracing the scales on her own arms, a strange, wistful feeling filling her chest. “I wonder if… if I’ll ever feel that way.”

He looked at her, his gaze softening, and for a moment, he seemed almost vulnerable. “Maybe,” he murmured. “If you let yourself.”

They sat in silence, the night quiet around them, and for the first time, she felt a faint sense of hope, a glimmer of something she hadn’t felt in days.

---

The morning of their departure dawned quietly, the grove bathed in the soft light of early dawn. Joni felt stronger, her mind clearer, the memories of Leir still there but no longer overwhelming, no longer threatening to consume her. She looked at K, her gaze steady, a faint smile tugging at her lips.

“Thank you,” she said, her voice filled with genuine gratitude.

He nodded, his expression serious. “Don’t thank me yet. We still have a long way to go.”

They set off, K leading the way, his steps sure and steady, his gaze fixed on the horizon. She followed, her heart pounding with a mixture of fear and excitement, her mind clearer than it had been in days.

As they walked, she felt a strange sense of peace, a quiet assurance that, no matter what lay ahead, she was no longer alone. And as she looked up at the sky, a faint smile on her lips, she allowed herself to believe, just for a moment, that maybe, just maybe, there was a way to reclaim herself from the fragments of Leir’s life, to forge a new path forward, one that was truly her own.

“Sure of what?” she asked, her voice barely more than a whisper.

He hesitated, his hand hovering just above her arm, his fingers brushing over the edge of her scales. “Sure that there was enough of *you* left to save.”

She looked away, the weight of his words pressing down on her, a mixture of gratitude and sadness welling up inside her. She felt as though she were slipping away, her own self lost somewhere between Leir’s memories and the physical transformation taking over her body.

“Rest here,” he said quietly. “I’ll stay nearby.”

---

When she woke, the sun was just beginning to rise, casting a soft, golden glow over the clearing. She sat up slowly, her body aching, her mind clearer than it had been in days. For the first time, she felt a flicker of her old self, a quiet resilience pushing through the fog.

But as she glanced around, searching for K, her breath caught.

In the early morning light, a dragon stood at the edge of the clearing, its scales gleaming like polished obsidian, catching the sun’s rays with a deep, shimmering brilliance. She nodded slowly, her fingers brushing over the scales on her own arms, a strange, wistful feeling stirring in her chest. “Sometimes… I feel like I’m starting to,” she admitted. “But it’s… unsettling.”

He watched her for a long moment, his gaze unreadable, and then he took a step closer, his hand resting gently on her shoulder. “You’re not trapped in this, Joni. You can… accept it, even if it changes you. You don’t have to lose yourself.”

The warmth of his hand on her shoulder steadied her, grounding her, and for the first time, she felt a faint sense of peace, a quiet, tentative assurance that maybe, just maybe, she could find a way to reconcile these two parts of herself.

Joni had long since lost track of time, each day in the dragon king’s fortress blending into the next. On the morning of their departure, K helped her to her feet, his arm steady around her shoulders, his gaze fixed on the path ahead. Her body felt stronger, her mind clearer, the memories of Leir still present but no longer consuming, no longer threatening to overtake her. She looked up at him, a faint smile tugging at her lips, her heart filled with gratitude.

They set off, K leading the way through the dense forest, his steps sure and steady, his presence a quiet reassurance in the early morning light. She followed, her heart pounding with a mixture of fear and excitement, her mind clearer than it had been in days.

As they walked, she felt a strange sense of peace, a quiet assurance that, no matter what lay ahead, she was no longer alone. And as she looked up at the sky, the sun casting a warm glow over the world around them, she allowed herself to believe, just for a moment, that maybe, just maybe, there was a way to reclaim herself from the fragments of Leir’s life, to forge a new path forward, one that was truly her own.

The forest around them was shrouded in silence, the only sounds the soft crunch of leaves beneath their feet and the faint rustling of the wind through the trees. Joni’s body felt heavy, her skin prickling with a strange, unfamiliar sensation. K watched her, his gaze filled with a quiet admiration, a faint, almost wistful smile tugging at his lips. “You did it,” he murmured, his voice soft, almost reverent. “You found your way back.”

She managed a faint smile, though her body was still trembling, her mind still reeling from the experience. “I… don’t know if I can keep doing that.”

She listened, her heart pounding, a mixture of fear and longing filling her chest. She could still feel the remnants of the transformation, the faint pulse of that draconic power within her, and a part of her wondered if she could ever feel that sense of belonging, that freedom.

“Do you think… I could ever feel that way?” she asked, her voice barely more than a whisper.

He looked at her, his gaze steady, his expression thoughtful. “Maybe,” he murmured. “If you let yourself.”

They sat in silence, the weight of his words settling over her, and for the first time, she felt a faint sense of peace, a quiet acceptance of the changes within her, of the transformation that was slowly becoming a part of her.

“That’s it,” he murmured, his voice filled with a quiet pride. “Heartspire. It’s… a sanctuary. A place where… where those who are lost can find themselves again.”

Everyone, he’d told her, had the ability to be anything, to become anything, if they could reach deep enough into themselves, if they could remember the essence of what they wanted to become. There were spells, dark magic, that forced people to remember things they’d rather forget—forms that felt unnatural, skins they could never shake off. That was how Maggie had become trapped in her dragon form: the spell had struck her with such brutal force, such sudden remembrance, that she had been forced into the transformation without a choice. She hadn’t meant to remember. But now, it was as if a door had been opened that couldn’t be closed.

Her spine stretched, wings sprouting from her back, their thin membranes taut and ready to catch the wind. Her senses flared to life, each sound and scent around her magnified, intense, almost overwhelming.

She opened her eyes, her vision sharpened, every leaf, every stone, every blade of grass clear and vivid. The world around her seemed new, alive in a way she had never experienced before. She felt powerful, connected to something vast and primal, as if she were tapping into an ancient memory that went beyond her own life, beyond even Leir’s.

K watched her with a quiet intensity, his eyes filled with understanding. Without a word, he shifted into his own dragon form, his dark scales glinting in the moonlight, his wings stretching wide as he crouched beside her, his presence steady, grounding.

“Let it happen,” he said, his voice a low, rumbling whisper that vibrated through the air. “This memory—this part of you—has always been there. Don’t fight it.”

She took a deep breath, closing her eyes, letting the memory of the dragon form settle over her like a second skin, sinking into the ancient rhythms, the primal instincts that pulsed through her. She could feel Leir’s memories, her experiences, blending with her own, as though the two of them were intertwined, one soul bound to another.

As she let herself drift into the memory, she realized something remarkable: she could feel the memory of how to become a dragon as if it were a natural part of her, something she had always known. The scales, the wings, the sharpness of her senses—all of it felt instinctual, familiar, as though she were remembering a part of herself she hadn’t known was there.

“It’s… like I always knew,” she murmured, her voice a low rasp. She glanced down at her hands—her claws—marveling at the strength in them, the sleek, dangerous beauty of the scales that covered her arms.

K nodded, his gaze softening. “We all know, deep down, how to be everything. It’s just a matter of… remembering.”

She looked up at him, her heart pounding, a mixture of fear and exhilaration filling her chest. “Then… why couldn’t I remember before?”

“Because some things… they need to be brought out,” he said softly, his eyes thoughtful. “Sometimes, we have to be forced to remember what we are capable of.”

She felt a pang at his words, the weight of the transformation settling over her. The realization of what she could become, of the memories and instincts hidden within her, was both thrilling and terrifying. She could feel the potential within her, the ability to shift, to change, to become something ancient and powerful. But at the same time, she knew that the transformation wasn’t something to take lightly, that the memory of being a dragon could consume her if she let it.

“K…” she whispered, her voice trembling. “What if I lose myself?”

He stepped closer, his dragon form towering over her, his eyes filled with understanding. “You won’t,” he murmured, his voice a steady rumble. “Because this is just one part of you. A memory. You’re more than this form, more than any memory.”

She closed her eyes, focusing on her own name, her own memories, grounding herself in her own identity, her own life. Slowly, the scales began to fade, her wings folding back into her body, her claws shrinking, her skin softening. She felt herself shrinking, returning to her human form, the memory of the dragon settling within her like a quiet, latent power, something she could call upon if she chose, but not something that defined her.

When she opened her eyes, she was herself again, her human skin smooth, her senses dulled to their familiar state. But she could still feel the dragon within her, the memory of it resting in her bones, a reminder of what she could become.

K shifted back to his human form, his gaze warm, approving. “You did it,” he said softly, a faint smile tugging at his lips. “You found your way back.”

She managed a small, shaky smile, the weight of the experience settling over her. “I… don’t know if I can keep doing that. It’s… overwhelming.”

He nodded, his expression thoughtful. “It is. But it’s part of you now. You can call on it if you need to. And if you don’t… it will wait.”

They rested by the stream, K keeping close, his presence a steady anchor as she grappled with the remnants of the transformation, the memories of flight, of power, that still lingered in her mind. She could still feel the draconic strength within her, a quiet reminder that she was capable of becoming more, of embracing that primal power if she needed to.

---

The following night, as they stood on a high cliff overlooking the valley below, K pointed to the sky, where a massive, glittering asteroid hung against the stars, its surface dotted with lights that twinkled in the darkness.

“That’s Heartspire,” he murmured, his voice filled with quiet reverence. “It’s… a sanctuary. A place where people go to find themselves, to… remember.”

Joni’s heart pounded as she looked at the asteroid, the glittering lights casting a soft, ethereal glow over the surrounding stars. It looked like a city carved from starlight, a place that seemed to belong to the cosmos itself, ancient and mysterious.

“But… how do we get there?” she asked, her voice barely more than a whisper.

K turned to her, his eyes gleaming with a faint, mischievous light. “We fly.”

Without another word, he shifted into his dragon form, his dark wings stretching wide, his scales shimmering in the starlight. He looked back at her, his eyes warm, inviting, and she felt a thrill of excitement, a faint, instinctive pull that stirred the remnants of her transformation.

Taking a deep breath, she let herself remember, let the memory of the dragon form rise within her, filling her mind, her body. She felt the scales unfurl over her skin, her wings stretching, her senses sharpening as she embraced the transformation once more. And when she opened her eyes, the world was sharp and vivid, her body alive with the thrill of the memory she had remembered, the ancient form that was hers to call upon.

Together, they launched into the night sky, their wings slicing through the air as they soared toward Heartspire. The stars surrounded them, close and brilliant, the vastness of space opening up around them. She felt a freedom, a sense of connection to the universe itself, as though she were part of something infinite, something that had always been within her.

They climbed higher, the cold air filling her lungs, the lights of Heartspire drawing closer with each beat of their wings. And as they flew, she felt a quiet peace settle over her, a sense of belonging that went beyond


Assistant:
words, a realization that she was more than just herself, more than just Joni or Leir. She was a creature of earth and sky, of flesh and scales, of memory and power.

When they finally landed on a ledge at the edge of Heartspire, she shifted back into her human form, her legs unsteady, her heart still pounding with the exhilaration of flight. K stood beside her, his gaze warm, his expression filled with a quiet pride.

“Welcome to Heartspire,” he murmured, his voice soft, reverent.

She looked at him, her heart filled with gratitude, a faint smile tugging at her lips. “Thank you,” she whispered, her voice filled with sincerity.

He nodded, his gaze steady, a hint of warmth in his eyes. “This is just the beginning. Heartspire is a place of remembering… of finding yourself.”

As they walked together through the ancient city, the lights of Heartspire casting a warm glow over them, Joni felt a quiet, unshakable certainty settle within her, a sense that, no matter what lay ahead, she was ready to face it. She was something more, something whole, and as she looked up at the stars, she allowed herself to believe that, maybe, she had found a place where she truly belonged.

The transformation had taken more from her than she’d anticipated. After Joni had returned to her human form, she felt drained, her mind dulled, her body fragile and sore. Even though she could still sense the memory of that dragon shape within her, like a smoldering ember ready to be reignited, she was wary of it, unsettled by the sheer power that had surged through her. The shift had left her feeling both exhilarated and lost, as though she were no longer entirely herself.

The two of them stayed in the clearing for several days, giving her time to rest and recover. K watched over her, his presence a steady comfort in the unfamiliar silence of the forest. He’d bring her food and water, sometimes sitting beside her in companionable quiet, sometimes shifting into his dragon form to stretch his wings in the open space. Occasionally, he would sit in his human form nearby, keeping his distance while she processed her transformation, letting her come to terms with it in her own time.

One morning, as she sat by the stream, splashing her face with cold water to shake the last remnants of sleep, she saw K watching her with a contemplative expression, his gaze flickering between her and the faint pattern of scales that still lingered on her forearms.

“Have you… tried it again?” he asked, his tone casual, though his eyes held an intensity she hadn’t noticed before.

She hesitated, glancing down at her arms, the scales shimmering faintly in the morning light. “I haven’t… I’m not sure I want to,” she admitted, her voice low. “It feels like something that I don’t fully understand. Like if I let it happen again, I might not come back.”

He nodded, his gaze thoughtful. “That’s fair. But I don’t think it’ll control you if you don’t let it. You have to want it for it to take over.”

She looked at him, a mixture of fear and curiosity stirring within her. “Is that why… transformation magic works? Because we all know, deep down, how to be everything?”

We’re all made up of fragments, echoes of things we’ve been or could become. Transformation is just… remembering. The right pull, the right force, it brings that memory to life.” He paused, his gaze darkening. “But not everyone remembers by choice. “Like Maggie,” she whispered, the memory of her friend’s transformation still fresh, painful.

He nodded, his expression solemn. “Like Maggie. Dragon magic is… powerful. It binds tightly to the soul, so tightly that once someone remembers, it’s almost impossible to un-remember.”

She shivered, tracing the faint scales on her arms, wondering what might happen if she called on the transformation again. Would it consume her? Bind her to something she didn’t fully understand? She felt both drawn to it and wary of it, uncertain of what lay within.

Several days passed, the quiet forest offering a strange, uneasy peace. Joni was still haunted by dreams—fleeting images of Leir’s life, of flying over mountains, of fire, of fierce love that had turned bitter over centuries. She would wake up unsettled, the feeling of wings and scales lingering, as though part of her mind was still trapped in that ancient memory.

K noticed her unease, his gaze softening each morning when he saw her distant expression, the haunted look in her eyes.

“We can stay as long as you need,” he said one morning, his voice quiet. “But Heartspire… it might help you understand what’s happening.”

Joni looked up at him, uncertainty flickering in her gaze. “Heartspire… that’s where you wanted to take me, isn’t it?”

He nodded, his gaze steady. “It’s a place of remembrance. For people like us… people struggling to find themselves, it’s a sanctuary. I think you’ll find what you need there.”

She managed a faint smile, though her heart was pounding. The thought of leaving the forest, of stepping out into the unknown, was both terrifying and exhilarating. But Heartspire was in space, an impossible height to reach without wings. And as much as she had felt that exhilarating freedom in her dragon form, she wasn’t ready to try flying yet. She wasn’t sure she could handle another transformation so soon.

“So… how do we get there?” she asked, her voice tinged with apprehension. “I don’t think I can… fly.”

K tilted his head, a mischievous glint in his eyes. “I was hoping you’d be ready for it, but there are other ways.” He paused, a faint smirk tugging at his lips. “One of them is a bit risky.”

She raised an eyebrow, wary but intrigued. “Risky how?”

He leaned closer, his voice low. “We could go back to the castle and… borrow a ship.”

She stared at him, her heart skipping a beat. “Go back? Are you insane?”

“Probably.” He shrugged, unbothered, his eyes gleaming with a reckless excitement. “But it’s the quickest way to get to Heartspire. And besides, my father won’t expect us to be foolish enough to go back.”

Joni took a deep breath, considering the plan. The thought of returning to the castle, to the place that had nearly broken her, was horrifying, but she could see the logic in his plan. They needed a way to get to Heartspire, and as risky as it was, stealing a ship from the castle might be their best option.

“Alright,” she said finally, her voice steady. “Let’s do it.”

K grinned, his excitement contagious. “Good. We’ll leave tonight.”

As they walked, Joni noticed K glancing around, his gaze lingering on the ancient statues, the elaborate engravings in the stone. He seemed both fascinated and wary, his steps a bit slower than usual, as though he were trying to absorb the weight of the place.

Neal caught her gaze, giving her a reassuring smile. “Heartspire can be a little overwhelming, especially for the uninitiated,” he said, his tone light but understanding. “But you’ll get used to it. It has a way of… showing you what you need to see.”

K shot him a dubious look, but there was a flicker of curiosity in his eyes. “So, what? It’s like some ancient cosmic therapist?” His voice was laced with sarcasm, but there was a hint of intrigue in his tone.

Neal chuckled, nodding. “Sort of. This place has its own magic. It’s like… a mirror. It helps you remember things about yourself, things that might be buried, hidden. Sometimes it even shows you who you could become.”

Joni felt a quiet thrill at his words, a mixture of excitement and fear stirring within her. She could still feel the memory of her dragon form lingering within her, the faint pulse of that power resting beneath her skin, a quiet reminder of what she could become. The idea of facing that memory, of delving deeper into it, was both exhilarating and daunting.

Maggie gave her a gentle nudge, a faint smile on her lips. “Heartspire helped me a lot when I first got here. It was… well, it wasn’t easy, but it helped me come to terms with what I’d become. I think… I think it might help you too.”

Joni nodded, her heart pounding, the weight of the place settling over her. She glanced at K, who was watching her with an unreadable expression, his usual bravado softened, a quiet intensity in his gaze.

“Are you… alright?” she asked, her voice soft, almost hesitant.

He looked at her, his expression thoughtful, as though he were trying to find the right words. “This place… it’s different,” he admitted, his voice low. “I’ve never been anywhere like it. It feels… strange.”

Joni smiled, a faint warmth spreading through her chest. “It’s okay to be out of your element,” she murmured, her voice gentle.

He nodded, managing a small, wry smile. “Yeah. I guess so.”

As they continued deeper into Heartspire, Joni felt the city’s energy growing stronger, a subtle pulse that seemed to resonate with her heartbeat, with the memory of the dragon form that lay within her. She could feel the presence of Leir, faint but steady, like a shadow lingering just beyond her reach. The memories were there, buried but alive, waiting for her to find them.

Finally, they reached a wide plaza at the heart of the city, the open space bathed in starlight, the edges lined with statues of ancient guardians, their faces solemn, watchful. The air was filled with a quiet, reverent energy, a sense of purpose that seemed to reach into her soul, drawing her forward.

Leo stepped forward, his gaze softening as he looked at Joni, his voice barely more than a whisper. “This is the place,” he said, his tone filled with quiet reverence. “The heart of Heartspire. It’s where… where everything begins.”

Joni took a deep breath, her heart pounding as she looked around, feeling the weight of the place pressing down on her, filling her with both awe and fear. She could feel the memories stirring within her, the fragments of Leir’s life, of the dragon form she had embraced. And she knew, deep down, that Heartspire was offering her something—a chance to remember, to understand, to embrace the parts of herself she had feared.

K stood beside her, his gaze steady, his expression solemn. He reached out, his hand resting lightly on her shoulder, his touch grounding, reassuring.

“Whatever you find here,” he murmured, his voice low but steady, “you don’t have to face it alone.”

She looked at him, her heart swelling with gratitude, a faint, trembling smile on her lips. “Thank you, K,” she whispered, her voice filled with sincerity.

He gave her a nod, his eyes warm, understanding. And as they stood together in the heart of Heartspire, surrounded by friends, by the silent, ancient city that seemed to watch over them, Joni felt a quiet, unshakable certainty settle within her—a sense that, no matter

The atmosphere in Heartspire’s plaza was heavy with the kind of silence that demanded reverence. Its wide stone space was littered with starlight and dust, and the coldness of the asteroid’s surface seeped into the bones of everyone who stood there, waiting. The statues that lined the plaza loomed, their faces angular and sharp, like guardians worn down by centuries, witnessing every soul who had come here searching for something they’d lost.

Joni stumbled slightly as she and K landed, the memory of the flight—a frantic leap into the unknown—still fresh in her mind. Her heart was pounding, and her body ached from the transformation, from the lingering strain of holding onto that dragon shape as they’d hurtled down from the ship. The faint shimmer of scales clung to her arms, a reminder that she could still become that fierce, scaled thing if she chose. She could feel that power resting just beneath her skin, a faint ember ready to flare to life if she called upon it.

The landing had hardly registered with her before she felt the piercing gaze of someone familiar. Maggie stood there, half-hidden in the shadows, her expression locked somewhere between shock and disbelief. She stared at Joni with wide, hollow eyes, and for a second, Joni’s heart twisted painfully. Maggie’s face, her sunken, drawn features, told a story of weeks spent believing her sister had been dead, all while grappling with her own transformation.

Joni swallowed, taking a cautious step forward, but Maggie remained frozen, her gaze flitting between the scales that shimmered faintly on Joni’s skin and her face, her eyes wide with something closer to horror than joy. The others—Neal, Leo, Brother—stood behind her, watching, their faces tight and pale. They looked at Joni like she was a ghost.

“Mags…” Joni’s voice cracked, the words almost dying in her throat. She forced herself to keep moving, each step feeling like a gamble, like approaching someone she’d never met. “Maggie, it’s… it’s me.”

Maggie didn’t respond right away, her mouth opening and closing in silent disbelief. She looked like she’d been hollowed out over the past few weeks, the sharpness of her cheekbones and the weariness in her eyes a testament to the grief she’d been carrying. Her lips pressed into a thin line, and her gaze drifted to the scales on Joni’s arms, the faint outline of wings that still lingered at her shoulders.

“Joni…” Maggie’s voice was barely more than a whisper, thick with unshed tears. “I thought… I thought you were dead. We all thought you were dead.”

Joni felt a lump rise in her throat, a mixture of guilt and sorrow filling her chest. “I know. I’m sorry… I tried to get back. It just—”

“You… you’re like me.” Maggie’s gaze lingered on Joni’s arms, a sadness, even a kind of bitterness flashing in her eyes. She lifted her own hand, the scales catching the dim light, her fingers elongated into talon-like claws. She took a shaky breath, her voice wavering. “But not… not like me.”

Joni glanced down at her arms, the scales fading back to smooth skin, but she knew Maggie was right. She could still shift, still return to her human form fully, while Maggie… she’d been trapped. Joni could see the strain in Maggie’s posture, the way her sister’s body clung to a shape it wasn’t entirely meant to hold. The forced transformation had altered her in ways Joni couldn’t begin to understand, and she could see it in Maggie’s haunted expression, the quiet acceptance of something irreversible.

“I… didn’t choose this, Maggie,” Joni murmured, her voice barely more than a whisper. “I didn’t mean for any of it to happen.”

Maggie’s face crumpled, a mixture of relief and sorrow flashing across her features as she looked away, her jaw clenched tight. “I begged them, Joni,” she said, her voice thick with desperation, with the echoes of countless pleas. “I begged them to send someone, to try to get you back, but they didn’t… they said it was a risk they couldn’t take.”

Her words hit Joni like a blow, a wave of guilt and anger twisting in her chest. She reached out, her hand trembling, and grasped Maggie’s shoulder, the weight of her sister’s grief settling over her like a mantle.

“I’m here now,” she whispered, her voice fierce, resolute. “I’m not going anywhere.”

Neal stepped forward, clearing his throat, his gaze shifting between them. He looked at Joni with a mixture of disbelief and tentative relief, as though he still wasn’t sure she was real. “How… how did you even make it out?” he asked, his voice steady but laced with shock.

Joni glanced over at K, who had been standing slightly apart, his gaze fixed on the towering statues around them, his expression guarded, as though he was trying not to meet anyone’s eyes. She could tell he felt out of place here, that the weight of Heartspire’s history and purpose pressed on him in a way he hadn’t expected.

“K helped me,” she said quietly, her gaze shifting back to Neal. “He… got me out of the fortress, and we made it here. Barely.”

Neal’s eyes narrowed, flicking to K with a look of wary respect, and he nodded. “Guess I owe you one, then.”

K shrugged, his mouth quirking into a faint, almost sardonic smile. “Don’t mention it. Heartspire wasn’t exactly on my itinerary, but… here we are.”

Leo stepped closer, his expression calm, though his eyes held a quiet intensity. He gave K a once-over, then nodded, something almost like approval flickering across his face. “Heartspire’s not an easy place to find, especially for someone… from outside.” His gaze softened slightly as he looked at Joni. “It’s good to have you back.”

Joni nodded, the warmth of his words settling over her, grounding her in the reality of the moment. But as she looked at the towering statues, the ancient buildings that stretched up toward the stars, a sense of purpose stirred within her, a feeling that this place held answers she hadn’t known she needed.

Maggie’s voice broke the silence, her gaze fixed on Joni, her eyes fierce, almost desperate. “We need to talk to the Wise Ones,” she said, her voice hard, unyielding. “They might be the only ones who can help us. If they’ll even listen.”

Joni looked at her, confusion flickering in her gaze. “The Wise Ones?”

Neal nodded, a faint grimace crossing his face. “The alpacas. Council of Heartspire. They’ve been around longer than almost anyone here, and they’re… well, they don’t take to outsiders easily.”

“They didn’t listen to me,” Maggie added, her voice bitter, her hands curling into fists. “No matter how much I begged, no matter how much I explained… they refused to do anything. They left you there.”

K shifted uncomfortably beside her, his gaze fixed on the ground, his jaw clenched. Joni could tell he felt the weight of her words, the sting of being labeled an outsider, and she felt a pang of sympathy. Heartspire might have been a sanctuary for people like her and Maggie, people who had been transformed against their will, but for K… it was a place that didn’t quite fit him, that treated him with the same indifference it had shown to Maggie’s pleas.

“We’ll talk to them together,” Joni said quietly, her voice steady, determined. “If they won’t listen to one person, maybe they’ll listen to all of us.”

Maggie looked at her, her expression softening, and she nodded, a faint, tentative hope flickering in her eyes. Neal and Leo exchanged a glance, a shared understanding passing between them, and they both gave her a nod.

K sighed, a faint smirk tugging at his lips, though there was a hint of something more—a quiet resolve that hadn’t been there before. “Guess I’m in this now,” he muttered, his voice wry. “Alpacas and all.”

Joni stifled a laugh, the absurdity of the situation grounding her in the midst of the tension. She gave him a grateful smile, her heart swelling with a mixture of gratitude and something else, something she hadn’t quite let herself feel.

Together, they turned toward the heart of Heartspire, where the Council waited, the faint hum of ancient magic resonating through the air like a distant heartbeat. Joni felt the memory of the dragon form stirring within her, a quiet, powerful reminder of what she could become, of the strength that lay hidden within her.

As they walked through the plaza, her friends by her side, Joni felt a quiet, fierce determination settle over her. She wasn’t just here to remember, to uncover the fragments of Leir that lay buried within her—she was here to make a difference, to fight for her sister, for herself, and for the future they all deserved.

And no matter what Heartspire held for her, no matter what memories it would awaken, she knew she was ready to face it, one step at a time.

The Council chamber was dimly lit, with flickering lamps casting uneven shadows across the stone walls. The chamber’s massive doors were adorned with intricate carvings of Heartspire’s history, scenes of towering alpacas and wise old sages, their eyes narrowed in silent judgment as they looked down upon all who dared to stand before them.

Joni felt small beneath the weight of their stares, her sister and friends clustered around her, tense but determined. K stood a little apart, his gaze fixed on the Council members with a mixture of defiance and disinterest. She could tell that being in Heartspire was testing him; he wasn’t used to places like this, places that held history and authority in every corner.

In the center of the chamber sat the Council of Heartspire: seven ancient alpacas, each more regal and inscrutable than the last. Their fur gleamed silver and white, their eyes dark and wise, like creatures who had seen centuries pass. They held themselves with a quiet power, an air of finality in the way they looked over the group, assessing them with each flick of an ear, each solemn blink.

And there, standing beside the alpacas, was the king of Heartspire himself.

He was a tall, angular figure, his face lined with age but sharp, his gaze sharp and skeptical. His robe was a rich, dark blue, embroidered with silver stars that shimmered faintly in the low light. His presence filled the room with a quiet authority, and Joni could feel his wariness toward them, his reluctance to trust outsiders—even more so, non-humans. She could feel his gaze linger on K and Maggie, his expression cold, mistrustful.

At his side stood his son, John, a figure less intimidating than his father, with softer features and an air of empathy in his eyes. John held himself with a quiet dignity, his posture relaxed but watchful, his gaze drifting between the Council, his father, and the group with concern. There was something about him that felt calm, grounded, a steady presence in a room filled with tension.

The Council’s head alpaca, a majestic creature with fur as white as snow and eyes as dark as the night, leaned forward, breaking the silence with a voice that was deep and resonant. “You seek sanctuary in Heartspire,” he intoned, his words slow, deliberate. “And yet, you bring with you… elements that Heartspire has long avoided.” His gaze drifted meaningfully to Maggie, Brother, and K, the weight of judgment clear.

Maggie shifted uncomfortably, her scaled hands curling into fists at her sides. Joni could feel her sister’s discomfort, the pain of once again being treated as something other, something unwelcome.

“We’ve come to ask for your help,” Joni said, her voice steady, though her heart was pounding. “There’s a war brewing with the dragon king. He’s… gathering power, and if he isn’t stopped, he’ll tear through the universe. Heartspire could be next.”

The king’s gaze sharpened, his expression hardening. “And what do you expect us to do about that?” His voice was clipped, dismissive. “Heartspire has kept itself secure by remaining uninvolved in outside affairs. We have no interest in entangling ourselves with dragon conflicts.”

John shifted beside him, his expression troubled. “Father, they’re here because they need help. Perhaps—”

“Enough, John.” The king’s tone was curt, final. He turned his gaze back to the group, his eyes narrowing. “You bring with you strange creatures—half-dragons and shapeshifters,” he said, the disdain in his voice evident. “You will not be permitted to stay within the city walls. Our people have enough to contend with as it is.”

Joni’s heart sank, the weight of his words pressing down on her. She had expected some resistance, but the king’s outright rejection stung, especially knowing what they’d risked just to get here.

“We have nowhere else to go,” Maggie said quietly, her voice strained, her eyes fixed on the ground. “And we’re not here to harm anyone.”

The king looked at her coldly, unmoved. “You will stay in the swamps, beyond Heartspire’s borders. That is where your kind belongs.”

Joni clenched her fists, anger and frustration rising within her, but before she could speak, John stepped forward, his gaze steady, his voice calm but firm. “Father, the swamp is dangerous. If we send them out there, it’s as good as abandoning them.”

“They’re not our responsibility, John,” the king replied, his tone icy. “They are strangers here. And Heartspire has always been wary of those who are… not like us.”

The Council members exchanged glances, their expressions unreadable, but none of them spoke against the king’s decision. It was clear they supported his judgment, their silent agreement a reminder of the long-standing caution Heartspire held toward the unknown.

John looked back at Joni and the others, sympathy clear in his gaze. “Then I’ll go with them,” he said, his voice unwavering. “If you’re determined to send them to the swamp, then I’ll make sure they’re safe.”

The king’s eyes narrowed, his face hardening. “This is unnecessary, John. Your place is here, with your people.”

“Maybe my place is with everyone, Father,” John replied, his tone respectful but resolute. “If there’s a chance to make peace, to reach an understanding, then that’s where I should be. Heartspire may be wary of them, but they are people just the same.”

A tense silence settled over the room, the weight of John’s defiance hanging in the air. The king’s jaw clenched, his gaze cold and calculating, but he finally relented, his voice clipped. “Fine. Go with them, if you insist. But don’t expect Heartspire to intervene if they bring trouble upon themselves.”

John inclined his head, a faint smile of gratitude touching his lips as he turned back to the group. Neal and Leo exchanged a look, a silent decision passing between them, and Neal nodded, his voice calm as he spoke.

“We’re going with them too,” he said, his tone firm. “We came here to stand by them, not to watch from the sidelines.”

The king’s gaze flicked to Neal and Leo, a faint flicker of disapproval in his eyes, but he said nothing. It was clear that Heartspire’s doors had been closed to them, that whatever help they might receive would be on the other side of its walls, in the swamp that stretched out around the city like a dark, waiting maw.

With a curt nod, the king dismissed them, his gaze shifting back to the Council as though they had already ceased to exist in his mind.

John led them out of the chamber, his footsteps steady as he guided them down a series of winding stone corridors. The walls grew colder, the light dimmer, and Joni felt the weight of rejection settling over her, the reality of their situation pressing down like a stone in her chest. They were on their own.

Once they were outside the city walls, the landscape shifted, the paved streets giving way to a dense, mist-covered swamp. The air was thick with moisture, and a faint, earthy smell filled her lungs as they moved through the twisting trees and gnarled roots. Shadows flickered at the edges of her vision, the faint rustle of unseen creatures echoing through the mist.

John glanced at her, his expression calm but reassuring. “The swamp… it’s not as dangerous as it seems, but it’s not exactly friendly, either. There are creatures here who were once like you and me, transformed over time.”

Joni shivered, the weight of his words settling over her. She could feel the faint pull of her dragon form, the memory of it lurking in the back of her mind, and she wondered if the creatures of this swamp had once felt the same—a quiet, relentless transformation, a memory they couldn’t shake.

Maggie walked beside her, her scaled hand brushing against Joni’s arm, her gaze distant. “The monsters here… they don’t trust humans. Most of them used to be human, but they’ve been changed, and they… well, they don’t forget easily.”

Joni nodded, a faint unease stirring within her. She could feel the weight of those transformations pressing down on her, the silent reminder of what lay within her, of what she could become if she wasn’t careful.

As they walked deeper into the swamp, John moved closer to her, his expression thoughtful. “My father sees them as a threat, but I’ve spent my life trying to make peace with them. They’re not monsters—they’re just… different.”

Joni gave him a grateful smile, the warmth of his words settling over her. “Thank you, John,” she said quietly. “For staying with us. It means more than you know.”

He nodded, his gaze softening. “Everyone deserves a chance,” he said simply. “And Heartspire… it’s better than this. I know it is.”

As they continued on, the swamp grew darker, the shadows deepening, the sounds of unseen creatures echoing through the mist. Joni felt the weight of the transformation within her, the memory of scales and wings pressing against her skin, a reminder of the changes she could no longer ignore.

But with Maggie, Neal, Leo, and now John by her side, she felt a quiet strength, a determination that went beyond fear. They would find a way through this swamp, they would find a way to fight the dragon king, and, somehow, they would find a place where they could belong.

Together, they would face whatever lay ahead, one step at a time.

John took a step toward his father, his voice hardening. “They’re not monsters, Father. They’re people—people who were forced into this by the same creature who threatens our world.”

, the warmth of her sister’s presence a steadying force in the midst of the tension. She could feel the memory of her dragon form still thrumming beneath her skin, a reminder of the power she now carried, the shape she couldn’t quite leave behind.

The council chamber was cold, heavy with a silence that felt almost oppressive, the kind that settled deep in the bones. Joni, Maggie, K, Neal, Leo, and Brother stood in a tight line before the Council of Heartspire and its king, who watched them with a sharp, unyielding gaze. The chamber’s dim lanterns flickered, casting long, wavering shadows against the stone walls, illuminating the intricate carvings that seemed to shift and move under the light.

Joni took a breath, trying to steady her nerves, her heart pounding with the urgency of what they had to say. She could feel the weight of the council’s attention pressing down on her, and the king’s cold gaze settled over them like a final judgment.

She stepped forward, her voice steady, though the tension in her chest was a steady, insistent beat. “Your Majesty, members of the Council… we’ve come because the dragon king threatens not just us, but every world. He’s gathering forces, drawing in power from places we don’t even understand.”

The king’s face remained impassive, his expression unmoved as his gaze swept over them with a mixture of disdain and suspicion. “And why, exactly, does this concern Heartspire?” His voice was cold, clipped. “We have remained secure by keeping out of the affairs of others. Your plight is not ours.”

K shifted beside Joni, his face hardening, his jaw tight. “It’ll concern you soon enough,” he muttered, his voice laced with frustration. “The dragon king isn’t going to stop with us.”

The king’s gaze snapped to K, his eyes narrowing. “You speak with a boldness that does not become you, dragon,” he said icily, his tone laced with disdain.

Joni took another breath, trying to keep her voice calm. “Please, Your Majesty,” she said, fighting to keep the desperation from her tone. “The dragon king forced us into this. He’s conscripting people, shaping them into his soldiers, into… into what he wants. He’s not going to stop until he has total control.”

A murmur passed through the council, the alpacas exchanging looks, their faces impassive but their eyes betraying a flicker of concern. Joni felt a small spark of hope—perhaps they were getting through to them, perhaps they were beginning to see the threat.

The king’s face, however, remained unreadable, his gaze cold and detached. He looked over each of them with a hard, calculated stare before settling back in his seat, his fingers steepled as he addressed them. “Heartspire has survived countless threats, countless wars,” he said, his voice a quiet, unwavering authority. “We do not bow to fear. We do not bend to the whims of outsiders.”

John, standing to his father’s right, took a step forward, his face tense, his expression pleading. “Father, they’re here because they’re trying to prevent a war that could destroy worlds. We can’t just turn them away.”

The king’s eyes flicked to John, and his face hardened. “You are young, John,” he said, his voice cold. “You think you see the truth, but you lack the wisdom to understand that these creatures bring danger with them. They are dragons, infected with the same taint that drives the one who seeks to conquer us all.”

Maggie’s face twisted with hurt, her hand clenching into a fist. “We didn’t choose this,” she said, her voice strained, the pain clear in her tone. “We’re not… we’re not like him.”

The king’s gaze lingered on her, his lip curling in a faint sneer. “And yet, here you stand,” he said, his voice filled with disdain. “Dragon-kin, bringing with you the very corruption that Heartspire has long protected itself against.”

Joni opened her mouth to respond, but the king raised a hand, silencing her. His gaze swept over each of them, and then, in a voice as cold as the stone walls around them, he spoke a single, ancient word—a word that thrummed with a dark, binding magic.

Before Joni could react, she felt a strange, electric shiver run through her veins, a pulsing warmth spreading through her body, as if something deep within her was being summoned against her will. She glanced at Maggie, whose face had gone pale, her eyes wide with fear, and then at K, who looked just as alarmed, his fists clenching as his body tensed.

“What… what are you doing?” Joni gasped, the pull growing stronger, her skin prickling as scales began to unfurl against her will.

The king’s gaze was cold, unyielding, as he watched them. “If you wish to prove that you are not monsters, then resist. Show us that the dragon’s taint has not taken root within you.”

Joni gritted her teeth, fighting against the magic that pulsed through her, struggling to hold on to her human form. But the pull was relentless, deep and ancient, a memory woven into her very being. She felt her body beginning to shift, her muscles tensing, her back arching as wings began to sprout, her senses sharpening, the transformation taking hold despite her resistance.

Beside her, Maggie let out a quiet, pained gasp as her form shifted, her scales thickening, her claws lengthening against her will. K’s face twisted in frustration, his body contorting as he too transformed, his wings stretching out, his dark scales gleaming in the dim light.

John’s face went pale, horror flashing in his eyes as he watched his father’s magic force the transformation upon them. “Father, stop this!” he shouted, his voice filled with desperation. “This is wrong! They’re not doing this by choice!”

The king’s gaze remained hard, his voice cold and unyielding. “Look at them, John. This is what they truly are—creatures of fire and blood, beasts who cannot control their own nature.”

Joni struggled to keep herself from fully shifting, her mind fighting against the wave of primal instincts that surged within her. The memories of flight, of raw power, tugged at her, but she forced herself to hold on, to cling to the fragments of herself that hadn’t yet succumbed.

Through gritted teeth, she looked at the king, her voice strained but defiant. “This… this doesn’t change who we are.”

The king’s expression twisted with disdain, his gaze cold and pitiless. “It changes everything. You come here as monsters, and yet you expect us to treat you as anything else. Heartspire will not harbor creatures who cannot even control their own forms.”

Maggie’s face was drawn, her eyes dark with pain, her voice barely a whisper as she struggled to keep herself steady. “We’re not… monsters.”

But the king ignored her, his attention turning back to John, his gaze filled with a cold, cutting fury. “You insist on defending them, John. Do you still see them as people? Or do you finally understand the danger they bring?”

John’s face hardened, a steely determination settling over him as he took a step toward his father. “I still see them as people,” he said, his voice steady, resolute. “They didn’t ask for this. You forced this on them to prove a point. They’re not monsters—this transformation, this power, it doesn’t define who they are.”

The king’s face twisted with anger, his voice rising. “And what would you know of power, of control, of the dangers that lurk in those who carry the dragon’s taint?” He gestured sharply toward Joni, Maggie, and K, his eyes flashing. “They are what they are, and Heartspire will not bend to protect them.”

John’s expression grew fierce, his gaze unyielding as he met his father’s eyes. “Then I’ll go with them,” he said firmly. “If they are forced out, if they are cast into the swamp, I’ll go with them. Someone has to stand with them. Someone has to show them that they’re not alone.”

The king’s mouth tightened, his face a mask of barely controlled fury, but he said nothing. Joni could feel the weight of John’s words, the fierce loyalty that had driven him to defy his father, to stand with them even in the face of exile.

Joni forced herself to stand tall, to ignore the ache in her body, the residual shudder of the forced transformation. She met the king’s gaze, her own eyes hard, unyielding.

“We’ll find a way to stop the dragon king,” she said, her voice steady, resolute. “With or without your help.”

The king’s gaze darkened, a flicker of something dangerous passing through his eyes, but he said nothing. With a sharp nod, he turned his back on them, dismissing them with a finality that left no room for negotiation.

The swamp around them pulsed with an otherworldly stillness, shrouded in mist and the eerie silence of ancient growth. They’d been in the swamp for what felt like hours, the cold, damp air clinging to their skin as they moved through dense, twisted trees, the ground soft with mud and moss. At last, Neal and Leo stopped, exchanging a nod. Neal raised his hands, his fingers weaving through the air in a familiar pattern, and with a faint shimmer, a structure began to take shape.

The yurt shimmered into view, its cloth walls a comforting shade of deep green, blending seamlessly with the swamp’s colors. Neal and Leo exchanged a look of relief, then turned to the others, gesturing them inside. Joni felt a warmth in her chest at the sight, a sense of home and sanctuary in this forsaken landscape.

Inside, the yurt was as familiar as ever, filled with small comforts and their scattered belongings. The lanterns cast a soft glow on the walls, and the smell of spices and old leather welcomed them, giving a sense of refuge. Joni slumped onto a pile of blankets, exhausted but grateful, while Maggie took a seat beside her, her gaze fixed on the space around them.

For the next several days, they remained there, nestled within the yurt, taking turns patrolling the perimeter and keeping a low profile in the swamp’s dense canopy. With the dragons searching for them, the forest’s cover and the yurt’s magic were their best protections. Outside, the swamp buzzed with a strange, unfamiliar life—small creatures flitted in the shadows, and the sounds of rustling leaves and faint chirps created an alien music around them.

But inside, there was a sense of peace, a quietness that allowed them to finally breathe. They took turns sitting together in the yurt, sharing stories, their voices low but filled with a warmth that eased some of the lingering tension. Joni found herself talking to Maggie about things she’d never thought to share—the strange sensation of her dragon form, the memories it stirred, and the fear and thrill of it all.

One evening, Joni and Maggie sat by the edge of the yurt’s doorway, the swamp quiet around them. They had shifted briefly into their dragon forms, the scales gleaming faintly in the soft light, their wings tucked close to their bodies. They sat in silence for a while, just feeling the weight of their dragon forms, the strange strength in their limbs.

“It’s… different, isn’t it?” Maggie murmured, her gaze fixed on the ground, her claws scraping lightly against the earth. “Feeling like this. I can’t even explain it… like a memory of something I’ve always known but never understood.”

Joni nodded, her own voice soft. “It’s like I remember… but not with my mind. More like my body remembers.” She looked down at her scales, the faint shimmer of green and silver catching in the dim light. “When I’m like this, it’s like the world makes sense in a different way.”

Maggie gave a small, sad smile, her claws curling slightly as she ran her hand over them. “I wish I could… I wish I didn’t have to feel this way,” she admitted, her voice barely more than a whisper. “I don’t want this. I never did.”

Joni placed a hand on her sister’s scaled shoulder, squeezing gently. “We’ll find a way, Mags. I don’t know how, but… we’ll make it so you don’t have to be this if you don’t want to.”

Joni would often catch them in quiet conversation by the yurt’s doorway, John listening patiently as K talked animatedly about their plans, his voice filled with a rare enthusiasm.

One evening, they all gathered around the yurt’s small fire, sharing a rare moment of quiet camaraderie. Neal and Leo were preparing tea, their movements precise and familiar, while John and K sat side by side, exchanging quiet comments and the occasional grin. Joni looked around at them, feeling a warmth fill her chest, a sense of belonging that she hadn’t expected in a place like this.

But the peace didn’t last long. Soon, signs of activity began to trickle through the swamp—a distant roar, the faint shimmer of dragon scales against the dark sky. At first, it was easy to ignore, to brush off as a simple scout passing by, but as the days went on, the presence of dragons became harder to dismiss. They seemed to linger at the edges of the swamp, their forms circling in the distance, as if searching for something—or someone.

One afternoon, an emissary from Heartspire arrived, their approach announced by the faint rustle of leaves and the soft tread of boots on swampy ground. The figure emerged from the mist, a tall, stoic woman with sharp features and the insignia of the Heartspire guard on her chest.

Neal stepped forward, his expression hardening. “Then we have to leave. If he’s threatening Heartspire, we can’t stay here and put the whole kingdom at risk.”

The emissary nodded. “I would advise it. The king will do what he must to protect his people.”

Neal and Leo exchanged a look, a silent agreement passing between them. They began gathering their things, their movements swift, efficient. “Everyone, get inside the yurt,” Neal instructed, his tone calm but urgent. “We’re leaving for space.”

Through the small windows, she could see the dark silhouettes of dragons descending on Heartspire, their forms massive and terrible, their wings casting long shadows over the kingdom’s stone walls. The dragons attacked with a ferocity that seemed almost personal, as if driven by the dragon king’s own fury, their fire illuminating the swamp in bursts of orange and red.

“Everyone stay inside,” Leo said, his voice barely a whisper, his gaze fixed on the forest canopy above. “They might not notice us here.”

They huddled together in the yurt, the sound of dragon wings and the distant rumble of flames filling the air, each of them tense, silent. Maggie looked at him, her eyes wide with a mix of hope and fear. “You can… use that? My wish?”

Leo nodded, his gaze softening as he looked at her. “A wish is powerful magic, especially one made with as much conviction as yours. If we can harness it, we might be able to create a protective field around the yurt, to stop the transformation magic from taking hold.”

They worked late into the night, their voices a steady, quiet rhythm as they wove the spell. The air grew thick with energy, the faint scent of herbs and magic filling the yurt. Joni watched them, a sense of awe settling over her as she saw the dedication, the determination in their movements. They were fighting for something larger than themselves, for a chance to protect each other, to stand against the dragon king’s power.

the tension. Joni found herself smiling as she watched them, the warmth of their laughter a balm against the weight of their situation.

And as the yurt hummed with the spell’s magic, as the forest grew quiet around them, they sat together in a circle, each of them feeling the weight of what lay ahead, but also the fierce, unbreakable bond that would carry them through.

The yurt was like a fragile sanctuary under the swamp’s dense canopy, its walls a barrier against the chaos roaring just outside. For days, they huddled inside as the dragons attacked Heartspire above, the sound of wings and fire echoing through the trees, the swamp illuminated in brief, terrifying bursts of light. Each dragon cry shook the ground, and even though they knew the yurt was enchanted and hidden, they felt every rumble, every roar like a thunderstorm threatening to split their refuge in two.

Joni spent her nights restless, caught between exhaustion and the uneasy feeling that at any moment, a dragon would discover their hiding place and bring its wrath down upon them. She knew the others felt the same tension; they all slept in shifts, always keeping someone awake to keep watch. But inside the yurt, they did their best to pass the time, trying to find moments of peace amid the constant threat above.

One afternoon, as the sound of dragons retreating echoed distantly, Neal and Leo gathered everyone around, their faces serious as they addressed Maggie.

“Maggie,” Neal began, his voice calm but laced with the weight of what he was about to say, “we’ve talked it over, and we think… there may be a way to use your wish, but it’s not something we can just do here.”

Leo leaned forward, his expression earnest. “It’s your wish, Maggie, and that’s the magic we’ll need to tap into. But this spell, if it’s going to work, has to be done… right in front of the dragon king. It’s the only way the energy of your wish will resonate enough to take hold and stop the transformations.”

The color drained from Maggie’s face, her hands clutching at her sides as she took in their words. “You’re saying I have to… perform this spell myself? In front of him?”

Neal nodded. “Yeah. But first, you’ll need to learn magic. Figure out how to channel your wish, and then how to create the spell itself. Once you’re ready… you’ll have to go down there and face him.”

She even shared fragments of the memories of Leir, the pieces that surfaced in her mind like half-remembered dreams.

And in return, John spoke about Heartspire, about his efforts to bring peace to the kingdom and the monsters who lived in the swamp. “My father doesn’t understand,” he said one evening, his voice thoughtful, almost sad. “He sees the creatures here as threats, as reminders of something he wants to keep out. But… they’re not monsters. They’re just… different.”

Joni smiled, nodding. “I think I understand that now. Maybe better than I ever did before.”

Their connection grew stronger with each passing day, a quiet bond that felt like a spark amid the darkness of the swamp. But that closeness brought its own complications. She couldn’t help but notice K watching them, his eyes lingering on her and John with a mixture of curiosity and something else, something that made her heart twist. One evening, as the dragons seemed to pause in their assault, Joni sat outside the yurt, her thoughts drifting as she watched the stars glimmer faintly through the swamp’s thick canopy. K joined her, silent at first, his presence a comfortable weight beside her.

Joni glanced at him, her heart beating a little faster. She could sense the unspoken question behind his words, the vulnerability he rarely let slip. “He’s… he’s easy to talk to,” she admitted, her voice quiet. “He understands what I’m going through. But that doesn’t mean…”

Joni smiled, the tension easing a little as she nudged him with her shoulder. “Don’t worry, K. I know I’ve got options.” She looked up at the stars, feeling the quiet warmth of his presence beside her. It was complicated, tangled, but in this moment, it felt like enough.
